<p><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States"><img alt="President of the United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/40px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/60px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/80px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2424" data-file-height="2425" /></a></p>

<li class="nv-talk"><b><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:TRseries&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:TRseries (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></b></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><b><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TRseries&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></b></li>

<p><b>Theodore Roosevelt</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/o?/ long 'o' in 'code'">o?</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'">?</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/?/ short 'e' in 'bed'">?</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">ROH</span></span></b>-z?-velt</i></a></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> October 27, 1858 – January 6, 1919), often referred to as <b>Teddy</b> or <b>TR</b>, was an American statesman, author, explorer, soldier, naturalist, and reformer who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">26th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, from 1901 to 1909. A leader of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republican Party</a>, he was a leading force of the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a>. Born a sickly child with debilitating asthma, Roosevelt embraced a strenuous lifestyle and successfully regained his health. He integrated his exuberant personality, vast range of interests, and world-famous achievements into a "cowboy" persona defined by robust <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a>. Home-schooled, he became a lifelong naturalist before attending <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a>. His first of many books, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Naval_War_of_1812" title="The Naval War of 1812">The Naval War of 1812</a></i> (1882), established his reputation as both a learned historian and a popular writer. He entered politics, becoming the leader of the reform faction of Republicans in New York's state legislature. Following the deaths of his wife and mother, he escaped to the wilderness and operated a cattle ranch in the Dakotas. He returned to run unsuccessfully for Mayor of New York City in 1886. He served as <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</a> under William McKinley, resigning after one year to serve with the <a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders" title="Rough Riders">Rough Riders</a>, gaining national fame for courage during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">War in Cuba</a>. Returning a war hero, he was elected governor of New York in 1898. A frustrated party establishment made him McKinley's running mate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">election of 1900</a>. He campaigned vigorously across the country, helping McKinley win reelection by a landslide on a platform of peace, prosperity, and conservatism.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_William_McKinley" title="Assassination of William McKinley">assassination of President McKinley</a> in September 1901 meant that at age 42, Roosevelt had become President of the United States, the youngest in history. Leading his party and country into the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a>, he championed his "<a href="/wiki/Square_Deal" title="Square Deal">Square Deal</a>" domestic policies, promising the average citizen fairness, breaking of trusts, regulation of railroads, and pure food and drugs. Making conservation a top priority, he established myriad new <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">national parks, forests, and monuments</a> in order to preserve the nation's natural resources. In foreign policy, he <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Corollary" title="Roosevelt Corollary">concentrated on Central America</a>, where he began construction of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>. He also greatly expanded the United States Navy and sent the <a href="/wiki/Great_White_Fleet" title="Great White Fleet">Great White Fleet</a> on a world tour to project the United States' naval power. His successful efforts to end the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> won him the 1906 Nobel Peace Prize.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1904" title="United States presidential election, 1904">Elected in 1904</a> to a full term, he continued pursuit of progressive policies, eventually culminating in blockage of his legislative agenda in Congress. Roosevelt successfully groomed his close friend, <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>, for the presidency. After leaving office, he went on safari in Africa and toured Europe. Returning to the USA, he became frustrated with Taft's approach as his successor, trying, but failing to <a href="/wiki/1912_Republican_National_Convention" title="1912 Republican National Convention">win the nomination in 1912</a>. He founded his own party, the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive "Bull Moose" Party</a>, and called for wide-ranging progressive reforms. The split allowed the Democrats to win the White House and Congress in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1912" title="United States presidential election, 1912">1912</a>; those Republicans aligned with Taft would control the Republican Party for decades.</p>
<p>Frustrated at home, Roosevelt led a <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt%E2%80%93Rondon_Scientific_Expedition" title="Roosevelt–Rondon Scientific Expedition">two-year expedition in the Amazon</a>, nearly dying of tropical disease. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, he opposed <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">President Wilson</a> for keeping the U.S. out of the war against Germany, and offered his military services, which were never summoned. Although he planned to run again for president in 1920, his health quickly deteriorated, and he died in early 1919. Roosevelt has consistently been ranked by scholars as one of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents" class="mw-redirect">greatest U.S. Presidents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> His face adorns <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rushmore" title="Mount Rushmore">Mount Rushmore</a> alongside those of Washington, Jefferson, and Lincoln.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_Age_11_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/TR_Age_11_Paris.jpg/170px-TR_Age_11_Paris.jpg" width="170" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/84/TR_Age_11_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/84/TR_Age_11_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="209" data-file-height="226" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_Age_11_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Theodore Roosevelt at age 11</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg/150px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg.png" width="150" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg/225px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg/300px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="552" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Theodore_Roosevelt.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt's coat of arms, featuring a rose bush in reference to the name: "Roosevelt" comes from the Dutch for "rose field"<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Theodore Roosevelt was born on October 27, 1858, in a <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Birthplace_National_Historic_Site" title="Theodore Roosevelt Birthplace National Historic Site">four-story brownstone at 28 East 20th Street</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York</a>. He was the second of four children born to socialite <a href="/wiki/Martha_Bulloch_Roosevelt" title="Martha Bulloch Roosevelt">Martha Stewart "Mittie" Bulloch</a> and glass businessman and philanthropist <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt,_Sr." title="Theodore Roosevelt, Sr." class="mw-redirect">Theodore Roosevelt, Sr.</a> He had an older sister, <a href="/wiki/Bamie_Roosevelt" title="Bamie Roosevelt">Anna</a> (nicknamed "Bamie"), a younger brother, <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Bulloch_Roosevelt" title="Elliott Bulloch Roosevelt">Elliott</a>, and a younger sister, <a href="/wiki/Corinne_Roosevelt_Robinson" title="Corinne Roosevelt Robinson">Corinne</a>. Elliott was the father of First Lady <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Anna Eleanor Roosevelt</a>, the wife of President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin Delano Roosevelt</a>. His paternal grandfather was of Dutch descent;<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> his other ancestry included English, Scots-Irish,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Scottish, Welsh, French, and German.<sup id="cite_ref-vought_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vought-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Thee (Theodore Roosevelt, Sr.) was the fifth son of businessman <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Roosevelt" title="Cornelius Roosevelt">Cornelius Van Schaack "C.V.S." Roosevelt</a> and Margaret Barnhill. Thee's fourth cousin, <a href="/wiki/James_Roosevelt_I" title="James Roosevelt I">James Roosevelt I</a>, who was also a businessman, was the father of President Franklin Delano Roosevelt. Mittie was the younger daughter of Major <a href="/wiki/James_Stephens_Bulloch" title="James Stephens Bulloch">James Stephens Bulloch</a> and Martha P. "Patsy" Stewart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPutnam1958ch_1.E2.80.932_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPutnam1958ch_1.E2.80.932-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Through the Van Schaacks Roosevelt is a descendant of the <a href="/wiki/Schuyler_family" title="Schuyler family">Schuyler family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt's youth was largely shaped by his poor health and debilitating <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>. He repeatedly experienced sudden nighttime asthma attacks that caused the experience of being smothered to death, which terrified both Theodore and his parents. Doctors had no cure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1981.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DnuzmvrqPvdIC.26pg.3DPA93_93.E2.80.93108.5D_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1981.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DnuzmvrqPvdIC.26pg.3DPA93_93.E2.80.93108.5D-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, he was energetic and mischievously inquisitive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPutnam195823.E2.80.9327_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPutnam195823.E2.80.9327-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> His lifelong interest in zoology began at age seven when he saw a dead <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seal</a> at a local market; after obtaining the seal's head, Roosevelt and two cousins formed what they called the "Roosevelt Museum of Natural History". Having learned the rudiments of <a href="/wiki/Taxidermy" title="Taxidermy">taxidermy</a>, he filled his makeshift museum with animals that he killed or caught; he then studied the animals and prepared them for display. At age nine, he recorded his observation of insects in a paper entitled "The Natural History of Insects".<sup id="cite_ref-environment_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-environment-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt's father significantly influenced him. His father had been a prominent leader in New York's cultural affairs; he helped to found the Metropolitan Museum of Art, and had been especially active in mobilizing support for the Union war effort. Roosevelt wrote: "My father, Theodore Roosevelt, was the best man I ever knew. He combined strength and courage with gentleness, tenderness, and great unselfishness. He would not tolerate in us children selfishness or cruelty, idleness, cowardice, or untruthfulness." Family trips abroad, including tours of Europe in 1869 and 1870, and Egypt in 1872, also had a lasting impact.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt191313_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt191313-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Hiking with his family in the Alps in 1869, Roosevelt found that he could keep pace with his father. He had discovered the significant benefits of physical exertion to minimize his asthma and bolster his spirits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPutnam195863.E2.80.9370_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPutnam195863.E2.80.9370-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands199725.E2.80.9326_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands199725.E2.80.9326-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> With encouragement from his father, Roosevelt began a heavy regime of exercise. After being manhandled by two older boys on a camping trip, he found a boxing coach to teach him to fight and strengthen his weakened body.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands199731_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands199731-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer191920_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer191920-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt later articulated the abiding influence of the courageous men he read about, including those in his family: "I was nervous and timid. Yet from reading of the people I admired—ranging from the soldiers of Valley Forge and Morgan's riflemen, to the heroes of my favorite stories—and from hearing of the feats of my southern forefathers and kinsfolk and from knowing my father, I felt a great admiration for men who were fearless and who could hold their own in the world, and I had a great desire to be like them."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands199728_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands199728-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg/220px-5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg/330px-5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg/440px-5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1906" data-file-height="1450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:5.5.12TeddyRooseveltTaxidermyKitByLuigiNovi3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt's taxidermy kit<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt was mostly <a href="/wiki/Homeschooling" title="Homeschooling">home schooled</a> by tutors and his parents. Biographer <a href="/wiki/H._W._Brands" title="H. W. Brands">H. W. Brands</a> argues that "The most obvious drawback to the home schooling Roosevelt received was uneven coverage of the various areas of human knowledge".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands200949.E2.80.9350_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands200949.E2.80.9350-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> He was solid in geography (as a result of self study during travels), and bright in history, biology, French, and German; however, he struggled in mathematics and the classical languages. He entered <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard College</a> on September 27, 1876; his father told him "Take care of your morals first, your health next, and finally your studies".<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After recovering from devastation over his father's death on February 9, 1878, Roosevelt doubled his activities. He did well in science, philosophy, and rhetoric courses but continued to struggle in Latin and Greek. He studied biology intently and was already an accomplished naturalist and a published ornithologist; he read prodigiously with an almost photographic memory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands199762_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands199762-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> While at Harvard, Roosevelt participated in rowing and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Boxing_Club" title="Harvard Boxing Club">boxing</a>; he was once runner-up in a Harvard boxing tournament. Roosevelt was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Delta_Phi" title="Alpha Delta Phi">Alpha Delta Phi</a> literary society, the <a href="/wiki/Delta_Kappa_Epsilon" title="Delta Kappa Epsilon">Delta Kappa Epsilon</a> fraternity, and the <a href="/wiki/Porcellian_Club" title="Porcellian Club">Porcellian Club</a>; he was also an editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Advocate" title="The Harvard Advocate">The Harvard Advocate</a></i>. On June 30, 1880, Roosevelt graduated <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa" class="mw-redirect">Phi Beta Kappa</a> (22nd of 177) from Harvard with an <a href="/wiki/Bachelors_of_Arts" title="Bachelors of Arts" class="mw-redirect">A.B.</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_cum_laude" title="Magna cum laude" class="mw-redirect">magna cum laude</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He entered <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a>, and was an able student, but he often found law to be irrational; he spent much of his time writing a book on the War of 1812. Roosevelt eventually became entirely disenchanted with law and diverted his attention to politics at Morton Hall on 59th Street, the headquarters for New York's 21st District Republican Association. When the members of the association encouraged him to run for public office, he dropped out of law school to do so, later saying, "I intended to be one of the governing class."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997110.E2.80.9312.2C_123.E2.80.9333._quote_p._126_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997110.E2.80.9312.2C_123.E2.80.9333._quote_p._126-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Naval_War_of_1812"><i>The Naval War of 1812</i></span></h2>
<p>While at Harvard, Roosevelt began a systematic study of the role played by the nascent <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">US Navy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, largely completing two chapters of a book he published after graduation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt191335_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt191335-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1979565_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1979565-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Assisted by two uncles, he scrutinized original source materials and official US Navy records. Roosevelt's carefully researched book, published in 1882, was comparable to modern <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_dissertation" title="Doctoral dissertation" class="mw-redirect">doctoral dissertations</a>, complete with drawings of individual and combined ship maneuvers, charts depicting the differences in iron throw weights of cannon shot between American and British forces, and analyses of the differences between English and American leadership down to the ship-to-ship level. Published after Roosevelt's graduation from Harvard, <i>The Naval War of 1812</i> was praised for its scholarship and style, and it showed Roosevelt to be a scholar of history. One modern naval historian wrote: "Roosevelt's study of the War of 1812 influenced all subsequent scholarship on the naval aspects of the War of 1812 and continues to be reprinted. More than a classic, it remains, after 120 years, a standard study of the war."<sup id="cite_ref-ijnh_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ijnh-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt summarized one of the primary morals of the war: "It must be but a poor spirited American whose veins do not tingle with pride when he reads of the cruises and fights of the sea-captains, and their grim prowess, which kept the old Yankee flag floating over the waters of the Atlantic for three years, in the teeth of the mightiest naval power the world has ever seen."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_marriage_and_widowerhood">First marriage and widowerhood</span></h2>
<p>On his 22nd birthday, Roosevelt married socialite <a href="/wiki/Alice_Hathaway_Lee_Roosevelt" title="Alice Hathaway Lee Roosevelt">Alice Hathaway Lee</a>, daughter of banker George Cabot Lee and Caroline Watts Haskell. Their daughter, <a href="/wiki/Alice_Roosevelt_Longworth" title="Alice Roosevelt Longworth">Alice Lee Roosevelt</a>, was born on February 12, 1884. Roosevelt's wife died two days after giving birth due to an undiagnosed case of <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure" class="mw-redirect">kidney failure</a> (called <a href="/wiki/Bright%27s_disease" title="Bright's disease">Bright's disease</a> at the time), which had been masked by the pregnancy. In his diary, Roosevelt wrote a large 'X' on the page and then, "The light has gone out of my life." His mother, Mittie, had died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> eleven hours earlier at 3:00&#160;a.m., in the same house. Distraught, Roosevelt left baby Alice in the care of his sister Bamie in New York City while he grieved. He assumed custody of his daughter when she was three.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt also reacted by focusing on work, specifically by re-energizing a legislative investigation into corruption of the New York City government, which arose from a concurrent bill proposing that power be centralized in the mayor's office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997166_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997166-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> For the rest of his life, he rarely spoke about his wife Alice and did not write about her in his autobiography. While working with Joseph Bucklin Bishop on a biography that included a collection of his letters, Roosevelt did not mention his marriage to Alice nor his second marriage to <a href="/wiki/Edith_Roosevelt" title="Edith Roosevelt">Edith Kermit Carow</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1979232_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1979232-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_NY_State_Assemblyman_1883_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/TR_NY_State_Assemblyman_1883_crop.jpg/170px-TR_NY_State_Assemblyman_1883_crop.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/48/TR_NY_State_Assemblyman_1883_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/48/TR_NY_State_Assemblyman_1883_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="230" data-file-height="284" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_NY_State_Assemblyman_1883_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt as NY State Assemblyman, 1883</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_Assemblyman">State Assemblyman</span></h3>
<p>Roosevelt was soon put forth as the Republican party's candidate for the District's House seat in Albany.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997126.E2.80.9329_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997126.E2.80.9329-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> He was a member of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Assembly" title="New York State Assembly">New York State Assembly</a> (New York Co., 21st D.) in <a href="/wiki/105th_New_York_State_Legislature" title="105th New York State Legislature">1882</a>, <a href="/wiki/106th_New_York_State_Legislature" title="106th New York State Legislature">1883</a> and <a href="/wiki/107th_New_York_State_Legislature" title="107th New York State Legislature">1884</a>. He immediately began making his mark, specifically in corporate corruption issues.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> He blocked a corrupt effort by financier <a href="/wiki/Jay_Gould" title="Jay Gould">Jay Gould</a> to lower his taxes. Roosevelt exposed suspected collusion in the matter by Judge Theodore Westbrook, and argued for and received approval for an investigation to proceed, aiming for the impeachment of the judge. The investigation committee rejected impeachment, but Roosevelt had exposed the potential corruption in Albany, and thus assumed a high and positive political profile in multiple New York publications.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997134.E2.80.9340_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997134.E2.80.9340-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1883, Roosevelt became the Assembly Minority Leader. In 1884, he lost the nomination for <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_New_York_State_Assembly" title="Speaker of the New York State Assembly">Speaker</a> to <a href="/wiki/Titus_Sheard" title="Titus Sheard">Titus Sheard</a> by a vote of 41 to 29 in the GOP caucus.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was also Chairman of the Committee on Affairs of Cities; he wrote more bills than any other legislator.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_election_of_1884">Presidential election of 1884</span></h3>
<p>With numerous presidential hopefuls to choose from, Roosevelt supported Senator <a href="/wiki/George_F._Edmunds" title="George F. Edmunds">George F. Edmunds</a> of Vermont, a colorless reformer. The GOP preferred New York City's <a href="/wiki/Chester_Arthur" title="Chester Arthur" class="mw-redirect">Chester Arthur</a>, who was known as a spoilsman. Roosevelt fought hard and succeeded in influencing the Manhattan delegates in the state convention at Utica. He then took control of the state convention, bargaining through the night and outmaneuvering the supporters of Arthur and James G. Blaine; he gained a national reputation as a key person in New York State.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt attended the <a href="/wiki/1884_Republican_National_Convention" title="1884 Republican National Convention">GOP National Convention</a> in 1884 and fought alongside the <a href="/wiki/Mugwump" title="Mugwump" class="mw-redirect">Mugwump</a> reformers; they lost to the <a href="/wiki/Stalwart_(politics)" title="Stalwart (politics)">Stalwart</a> faction, who nominated <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a>. In a crucial moment of his budding political career, Roosevelt resisted the demand of the Mugwumps that he bolt from Blaine. He bragged about his one small success: "We achieved a victory in getting up a combination to beat the Blaine nominee for temporary chairman... To do this needed a mixture of skill, boldness and energy... to get the different factions to come in... to defeat the common foe."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997171_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997171-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also impressed by an invitation to speak before an audience of ten thousand, the largest up to that date. Having gotten a taste of national politics, Roosevelt felt less aspiration for advocacy on the state level; he then retired to his new "Chimney Butte Ranch" on the Little Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt refused to join other Mugwumps in supporting <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>, the governor of New York and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Democratic_Party" title="History of the United States Democratic Party">Democratic</a> nominee in the general election. He debated the pros and cons of staying loyal with his political friend, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a>. After Blaine won the nomination, Roosevelt had carelessly said that he would give "hearty support to any decent Democrat". He distanced himself from the promise, saying that it had not been meant "for publication".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPringle195661_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPringle195661-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> When a reporter asked if he would support Blaine, Roosevelt replied, "That question I decline to answer. It is a subject I do not care to talk about."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> In the end, he realized that he had to support Blaine to maintain his role in the GOP, and he did so in a press release on July 19.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1886, Roosevelt was the Republican candidate for mayor of New York City, portraying himself as "The Cowboy of the Dakotas". GOP precinct workers warned voters that the independent radical candidate <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a> was leading and that Roosevelt would lose, thus causing a last-minute defection of GOP voters to the Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/Abram_Hewitt" title="Abram Hewitt">Abram Hewitt</a>. Roosevelt took third place with 27% (60,435 votes). Hewitt won with 41% (90,552 votes), and George was held to 31% (68,110 votes).<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cowboy_in_Dakota">Cowboy in Dakota</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg/170px-TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg/255px-TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg/340px-TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg 2x" data-file-width="476" data-file-height="596" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_Buckskin_Tiffany_Knife.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Theodore Roosevelt as <a href="/wiki/Badlands" title="Badlands">Badlands</a> hunter in 1885. New York studio photo</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt built a second ranch named Elk Horn, thirty-five miles (56&#160;km) north of the boomtown of <a href="/wiki/Medora,_North_Dakota" title="Medora, North Dakota">Medora, North Dakota</a>. On the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Little_Missouri_River_(North_Dakota)" title="Little Missouri River (North Dakota)">Little Missouri</a>, Roosevelt learned to ride western style, rope and hunt; though he earned the respect of the authentic cowboys, they were not overly impressed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997182_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997182-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> However, he identified with the herdsman of history, a man he said possesses, "few of the emasculated, milk-and-water moralities admired by the pseudo-philanthropists; but he does possess, to a very high degree, the stern, manly qualities that are invaluable to a nation".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> He reoriented, and began writing about frontier life for national magazines; he also published three books – <i>Hunting Trips of a Ranchman</i>, <i>Ranch Life and the Hunting-Trail</i>, and <i>The Wilderness Hunter</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997191_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997191-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As a deputy sheriff, Roosevelt pursued three outlaws who had stolen his riverboat and escaped north up the Little Missouri. He captured them, but decided against a vigilante hanging; instead, he sent his foreman back by boat, and conveyed the thieves to <a href="/wiki/Dickinson,_North_Dakota" title="Dickinson, North Dakota">Dickinson</a> for trial. He assumed guard over them for forty hours without sleep, while reading <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> to keep himself awake. When he ran out of his own books, he read a <a href="/wiki/Dime_novel" title="Dime novel">dime store western</a> that one of the thieves was carrying.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> On another occasion, while searching for a group of relentless horse thieves, Roosevelt met <a href="/wiki/Seth_Bullock" title="Seth Bullock">Seth Bullock</a>, the famous sheriff of <a href="/wiki/Deadwood,_South_Dakota" title="Deadwood, South Dakota">Deadwood, South Dakota</a>. The two would remain friends for life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1979241.E2.80.9345.2C_247.E2.80.9350_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1979241.E2.80.9345.2C_247.E2.80.9350-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt brought his desire to address the common interests of citizens to the west. He successfully led efforts to organize ranchers to address the problems of overgrazing and other shared concerns; his work resulted in the formation of the Little Missouri Stockmen's Association. He was also compelled to coordinate conservation efforts and was able to form the <a href="/wiki/Boone_and_Crockett_Club" title="Boone and Crockett Club">Boone and Crockett Club</a>, whose primary goal was the conservation of large game animals and their habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997189_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997189-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> After the uniquely severe US <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_1886%E2%80%931887" title="Winter of 1886–1887" class="mw-redirect">winter of 1886–1887</a> wiped out his herd of cattle and those of his competitors, and with it over half of his $80,000 investment, Roosevelt returned to the East.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1979376_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1979376-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_marriage">Second marriage</span></h2>
<p>On December 2, 1886, Roosevelt married his childhood and family friend, Edith Kermit Carow (August 6, 1861 – September 30, 1948), a daughter of Charles Carow and Gertrude Elizabeth Tyler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer19194.2C_6_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer19194.2C_6-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The couple married at <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s,_Hanover_Square" title="St George's, Hanover Square">St George's, Hanover Square</a> in London, England. English diplomat <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Spring_Rice" title="Cecil Spring Rice">Cecil Arthur Spring Rice</a>, Roosevelt's close friend, served as best man.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The couple honeymooned in Europe, and while there, Roosevelt led a group to the summit of <a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc" title="Mont Blanc">Mont Blanc</a>, an achievement that resulted in his induction into the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society of London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> They had five children: <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Jr." title="Theodore Roosevelt Jr." class="mw-redirect">Theodore "Ted" III</a> (1887–1944), <a href="/wiki/Kermit_Roosevelt" title="Kermit Roosevelt">Kermit</a> (1889–1943), <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Roosevelt_Derby" title="Ethel Roosevelt Derby">Ethel</a> (1891–1977), <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Roosevelt" title="Archibald Roosevelt">Archibald</a> (1894–1979), and <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Roosevelt" title="Quentin Roosevelt">Quentin</a> (1897–1918). At the time of Ted's birth, Roosevelt was both eager and worried for Edith after losing his first wife, Alice, shortly after childbirth.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reentering_public_life">Reentering public life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_Service_Commission">Civil Service Commission</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1888" title="U.S. presidential election, 1888" class="mw-redirect">1888 presidential election</a>, Roosevelt successfully campaigned, primarily in the Midwest, for <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a>. President Harrison appointed Roosevelt to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">United States Civil Service Commission</a>, where he served until 1895. While in office, Roosevelt vigorously fought the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoilsmen</a> and demanded enforcement of civil service laws.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1919ch._VI.2C_pp._1.E2.80.932_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1919ch._VI.2C_pp._1.E2.80.932-54"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i>New York Sun</i> then described Roosevelt as "irrepressible, belligerent, and enthusiastic".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2F.3Fid.3DKPMEAAAAYAAJ.26printsec.3Dfrontcover.26dq.3DTheodore.2BRoosevelt.2Band.2BHis.2BTime.2BShown.2Bin.2BHis.2BOwn.2BLetters.23PPA2.2CM1_51.5D_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2F.3Fid.3DKPMEAAAAYAAJ.26printsec.3Dfrontcover.26dq.3DTheodore.2BRoosevelt.2Band.2BHis.2BTime.2BShown.2Bin.2BHis.2BOwn.2BLetters.23PPA2.2CM1_51.5D-55"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite Roosevelt's support for Harrison's reelection bid in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1892" title="U.S. presidential election, 1892" class="mw-redirect">presidential election of 1892</a>, the eventual winner, Grover Cleveland (a <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Democrat" title="Bourbon Democrat">Bourbon Democrat</a>), reappointed him to the same post.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2F.3Fid.3DKPMEAAAAYAAJ.26printsec.3Dfrontcover.26dq.3DTheodore.2BRoosevelt.2Band.2BHis.2BTime.2BShown.2Bin.2BHis.2BOwn.2BLetters.23PPA2.2CM1_53.5D_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2F.3Fid.3DKPMEAAAAYAAJ.26printsec.3Dfrontcover.26dq.3DTheodore.2BRoosevelt.2Band.2BHis.2BTime.2BShown.2Bin.2BHis.2BOwn.2BLetters.23PPA2.2CM1_53.5D-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's close friend and biographer, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bucklin_Bishop" title="Joseph Bucklin Bishop">Joseph Bucklin Bishop</a>, described his assault on the spoils system:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The very citadel of spoils politics, the hitherto impregnable fortress that had existed unshaken since it was erected on the foundation laid by Andrew Jackson, was tottering to its fall under the assaults of this audacious and irrepressible young man... Whatever may have been the feelings of the (fellow Republican party) President (Harrison)—and there is little doubt that he had no idea when he appointed Roosevelt that he would prove to be so veritable a bull in a china shop—he refused to remove him and stood by him firmly till the end of his term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2F.3Fid.3DKPMEAAAAYAAJ.26printsec.3Dfrontcover.26dq.3DTheodore.2BRoosevelt.2Band.2BHis.2BTime.2BShown.2Bin.2BHis.2BOwn.2BLetters.23PPA2.2CM1_51.5D_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2F.3Fid.3DKPMEAAAAYAAJ.26printsec.3Dfrontcover.26dq.3DTheodore.2BRoosevelt.2Band.2BHis.2BTime.2BShown.2Bin.2BHis.2BOwn.2BLetters.23PPA2.2CM1_51.5D-55"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_City_Police_Commissioner">New York City Police Commissioner</span></h3>
<p>In 1894, a group of reform Republicans approached Roosevelt about running for Mayor of New York again; he declined, mostly due to his wife's resistance to being removed from the Washington social set. Soon after he declined, he realized that he had missed an opportunity to reinvigorate a dormant political career. He retreated to the Dakotas for a time; his wife Edith regretted her role in the decision and vowed that there would be no repeat of it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997265.E2.80.9368_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997265.E2.80.9368-57"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tr_-_nyc_police_commissioner_1894_-_jacob_riis_bio_-_the_making_of_an_american_-_illustration_named_one_was_sitting_asleep_on_a_buttertub_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Tr_-_nyc_police_commissioner_1894_-_jacob_riis_bio_-_the_making_of_an_american_-_illustration_named_one_was_sitting_asleep_on_a_buttertub_crop.jpg/170px-Tr_-_nyc_police_commissioner_1894_-_jacob_riis_bio_-_the_making_of_an_american_-_illustration_named_one_was_sitting_asleep_on_a_buttertub_crop.jpg" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/Tr_-_nyc_police_commissioner_1894_-_jacob_riis_bio_-_the_making_of_an_american_-_illustration_named_one_was_sitting_asleep_on_a_buttertub_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/Tr_-_nyc_police_commissioner_1894_-_jacob_riis_bio_-_the_making_of_an_american_-_illustration_named_one_was_sitting_asleep_on_a_buttertub_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="279" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tr_-_nyc_police_commissioner_1894_-_jacob_riis_bio_-_the_making_of_an_american_-_illustration_named_one_was_sitting_asleep_on_a_buttertub_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NYC Police Commissioner Roosevelt walks the beat with journalist <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Riis" title="Jacob Riis">Jacob Riis</a> in 1894—Illustration from Riis' autobiography.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt became president of the board of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Police_Commissioner" title="New York City Police Commissioner">New York City Police Commissioners</a> for two years in 1895 and radically reformed the police force. The New York Police Department (<a href="/wiki/NYPD" title="NYPD" class="mw-redirect">NYPD</a>) was reputed as one of the most corrupt in America; the NYPD's history division records that Roosevelt was "an iron-willed leader of unimpeachable honesty, (who) brought a reforming zeal to the New York City Police Commission in 1895".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt implemented regular inspections of firearms and annual physical exams; he appointed 1,600 recruits based on their physical and mental qualifications, regardless of political affiliation, established <a href="/wiki/Meritorious_Service_Medal_(United_States)" title="Meritorious Service Medal (United States)">Meritorious Service Medals</a> and closed corrupt police hostelries. During his tenure, a Municipal Lodging House was established by the Board of Charities, and Roosevelt required officers to register with the Board; he also had telephones installed in station houses.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1894, Roosevelt met <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Riis" title="Jacob Riis">Jacob Riis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Muckraker" title="Muckraker">muckraking</a> <i>Evening Sun</i> newspaper journalist who was opening the eyes of New Yorkers to the terrible conditions of the city's millions of poor immigrants with such books as <i><a href="/wiki/How_the_Other_Half_Lives" title="How the Other Half Lives">How the Other Half Lives</a>.</i> Riis described how his book affected Roosevelt:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>When Roosevelt read [my] book, he came... No one ever helped as he did. For two years we were brothers in (New York City's crime-ridden) <a href="/wiki/Mulberry_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Mulberry Street (Manhattan)">Mulberry Street</a>. When he left I had seen its golden age... There is very little ease where Theodore Roosevelt leads, as we all of us found out. The lawbreaker found it out who predicted scornfully that he would "knuckle down to politics the way they all did", and lived to respect him, though he swore at him, as the one of them all who was stronger than pull... that was what made the age golden, that for the first time a moral purpose came into the street. In the light of it everything was transformed.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Roosevelt made a habit of walking officers' <a href="/wiki/Beat_(police)" title="Beat (police)">beats</a> late at night and early in the morning to make sure that they were on duty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997277_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997277-61"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> He made a concerted effort to uniformly enforce New York's <a href="/wiki/Blue_law" title="Blue law">Sunday closing law</a>; in this, he ran up against boss Tom Platt as well as <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a>—he was notified that the Police Commission was being legislated out of existence. Roosevelt chose to defer rather than split with his party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997293_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997293-62"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> As Governor of New York State before becoming Vice President in March 1901, Roosevelt signed an act replacing the Police Commissioners with a single Police Commissioner.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_as_a_national_figure">Emergence as a national figure</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</span></h3>
<p>Roosevelt had demonstrated, through his research and writing, a fascination with naval history; President William McKinley, urged by Roosevelt's close friend Congressman Henry Cabot Lodge, appointed Roosevelt as the <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</a> in 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Secretary of the Navy <a href="/wiki/John_D._Long" title="John D. Long" class="mw-redirect">John D. Long</a> was more concerned about formalities than functions, was in poor health, and left major decisions to Roosevelt. Roosevelt seized the opportunity and began pressing his national security views regarding the Pacific and the Caribbean on McKinley. Roosevelt was particularly adamant that Spain be ejected from Cuba, to foster the latter's independence and to demonstrate the U.S. resolve to reenforce the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997310.E2.80.9312_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997310.E2.80.9312-65"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Ten days after the battleship <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Maine_(ACR-1)" title="USS Maine (ACR-1)">Maine</a></i> exploded in the harbor of Havana, Cuba, the Secretary left the office and Roosevelt became Acting Secretary for four hours. Roosevelt cabled the Navy worldwide to prepare for war, ordered ammunition and supplies, brought in experts and went to Congress asking for the authority to recruit as many sailors as he wanted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997325.E2.80.9326_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997325.E2.80.9326-66"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was instrumental in preparing the Navy for the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>. Roosevelt had an analytical mind, even as he was itching for war. He explained his priorities to one of the Navy's planners in late 1897:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I would regard war with Spain from two viewpoints: first, the advisability on the grounds both of humanity and self-interest of interfering on behalf of the Cubans, and of taking one more step toward the complete freeing of America from European dominion; second, the benefit done our people by giving them something to think of which is not material gain, and especially the benefit done our military forces by trying both the Navy and Army in actual practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt2001157.E2.80.9358_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt2001157.E2.80.9358-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg/170px-Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg/255px-Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg/340px-Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="1300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Rooseveltnewtry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Col. Theodore Roosevelt</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_in_Cuba">War in Cuba</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders" title="Rough Riders">Rough Riders</a></div>
<p>Prior to his service in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>, Roosevelt had already seen reserve military service from 1882 to 1886 with the <a href="/wiki/New_York_National_Guard" title="New York National Guard" class="mw-redirect">New York National Guard</a>. Commissioned on August 1, 1882 as a <a href="/wiki/2nd_Lieutenant" title="2nd Lieutenant" class="mw-redirect">2nd Lieutenant</a> with B Company, 8th Regiment, he was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(U.S._Army)" title="Captain (U.S. Army)" class="mw-redirect">Captain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Company_(military_unit)" title="Company (military unit)">company</a> commander a year later, and he remained in command until he resigned his commission.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NPS_timeline_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS_timeline-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NatlGuard_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGuard-71"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TRAlmanac_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRAlmanac-72"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When the United States and Spain declared war against each other in late April 1898, Roosevelt resigned from his civilian leadership job with the Navy on May 6 and formed the <a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders" title="Rough Riders">First US Volunteer Cavalry Regiment</a> along with Army Colonel <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Wood" title="Leonard Wood">Leonard Wood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-73"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Referred to by the press as the "Rough Riders", the regiment was one of many temporary units active only for the duration of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuels1997148_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuels1997148-74"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After securing modern multiple-round Krag smokeless carbines, Lieutenant Colonel Roosevelt arrived on May 15.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_timeline_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS_timeline-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The regiment trained for several weeks in San Antonio, Texas, and in his autobiography Roosevelt wrote that his prior National Guard experience had been invaluable, in that it enabled him to immediately begin teaching his men basic soldiering skills.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The Rough Riders used some standard issue gear and some of their own design, purchased with gift money. Diversity characterized the regiment, which included Ivy Leaguers, professional and amateur athletes, upscale gentlemen as well as cowboys, frontiersmen, Native Americans, hunters, miners, prospectors, former soldiers, tradesmen, and sheriffs. The Rough Riders were part of the cavalry division commanded by former Confederate general <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wheeler" title="Joseph Wheeler">Joseph Wheeler</a>. It was one of three divisions in the V Corps under Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/William_Rufus_Shafter" title="William Rufus Shafter">William Rufus Shafter</a>. Roosevelt and his men departed Tampa on June 13, landed in <a href="/wiki/Daiquiri" title="Daiquiri">Daiquiri</a>, Cuba, on June 23, 1898, and marched to Siboney. Wheeler sent parts of the 1st and 10th Regular Cavalry on the lower road northwest and sent the "Rough Riders" on the parallel road running along a ridge up from the beach. To throw off his infantry rival, Wheeler left one regiment of his Cavalry Division, the 9th, at Siboney so that he could claim that his move north was only a limited reconnaissance if things went wrong. Roosevelt was promoted to colonel and took command of the regiment when Wood was put in command of the brigade.</p>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Guasimas" title="Battle of Las Guasimas">Battle of Las Guasimas</a></div>
<p>The Rough Riders had a short, minor skirmish known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Guasimas" title="Battle of Las Guasimas">Battle of Las Guasimas</a>; they fought their way through Spanish resistance and, together with the Regulars, forced the Spaniards to abandon their positions.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Rough_Riders_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Rough_Riders-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Juan_Hill" title="Battle of San Juan Hill">Battle of San Juan Hill</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg/220px-TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg/330px-TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg/440px-TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1144" data-file-height="925" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_San_Juan_Hill_1898.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Colonel Roosevelt and the Rough Riders after capturing Kettle Hill along with members of the 3rd Volunteers and the regular Army black <a href="/wiki/10th_Cavalry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="10th Cavalry Regiment (United States)">10th Cavalry</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under his leadership, the Rough Riders became famous for the charge up <a href="/wiki/Kettle_Hill" title="Kettle Hill" class="mw-redirect">Kettle Hill</a> on July 1, 1898, while supporting the regulars. Roosevelt had the only horse, and rode back and forth between rifle pits at the forefront of the advance up Kettle Hill, an advance that he urged despite the absence of any orders from superiors. He was forced to walk up the last part of Kettle Hill, because his horse had been entangled in barbed wire. The victories came at a cost of 200 killed and 1000 wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997356_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997356-77"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt commented on his role in the battles: "On the day of the big fight I had to ask my men to do a deed that European military writers consider utterly impossible of performance, that is, to attack over open ground an unshaken infantry armed with the best modern repeating rifles behind a formidable system of entrenchments. The only way to get them to do it in the way it had to be done was to lead them myself."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997357_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997357-78"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roosevelt_as_a_veteran">Roosevelt as a veteran</span></h4>
<p>In August, Roosevelt and other officers demanded that the soldiers be returned home. Roosevelt always recalled the Battle of Kettle Hill (part of the San Juan Heights) as "the great day of my life" and "my crowded hour". In 2001, Roosevelt was posthumously awarded the <a href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor" title="Medal of Honor">Medal of Honor</a> for his actions; he had been nominated during the war, but Army officials, annoyed at his grabbing the headlines, blocked it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuels1997.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D1v7PSGbzhDwC.26pg.3DPA266_266.5D_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuels1997.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D1v7PSGbzhDwC.26pg.3DPA266_266.5D-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> After returning to civilian life, Roosevelt preferred to be known as "Colonel Roosevelt" or "The Colonel". However, "Teddy" remained much more popular with the public, even though Roosevelt openly despised it. Men working closely with Roosevelt customarily called him "Colonel" or "Theodore".<sup id="cite_ref-HandyBook_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HandyBook-80"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_New_York">Governor of New York</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TR-Cowboy.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3e/TR-Cowboy.JPG/200px-TR-Cowboy.JPG" width="200" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3e/TR-Cowboy.JPG/300px-TR-Cowboy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3e/TR-Cowboy.JPG/400px-TR-Cowboy.JPG 2x" data-file-width="579" data-file-height="804" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TR-Cowboy.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chicago newspaper sees cowboy-TR campaigning for governor</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After leaving the Army, Roosevelt discovered that New York Republicans needed him, because their current governor was tainted by scandal and would probably lose. He campaigned vigorously on his war record, winning the <a href="/wiki/New_York_state_election,_1898" title="New York state election, 1898">1898 state election</a> by a historical margin of 1%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1979674.E2.80.9387_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1979674.E2.80.9387-81"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As governor, Roosevelt learned much about ongoing economic issues and political techniques that later proved valuable in his presidency. He was exposed to the problems of trusts, monopolies, labor relations, and conservation. Chessman argues that Roosevelt's program "rested firmly upon the concept of the square deal by a neutral state". The rules for the Square Deal were "honesty in public affairs, an equitable sharing of privilege and responsibility, and subordination of party and local concerns to the interests of the state at large".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChessman19656_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChessman19656-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By holding twice-daily press conferences—which was an innovation—Roosevelt remained connected with his middle-class political base.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris1997693_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris1997693-83"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt successfully pushed the Ford Franchise-Tax bill, which taxed public franchises granted by the state and controlled by corporations, declaring that "a corporation which derives its powers from the State, should pay to the State a just percentage of its earnings as a return for the privileges it enjoys".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> He rejected "boss" <a href="/wiki/Thomas_C._Platt" title="Thomas C. Platt">Thomas C. Platt</a>'s worries that this approached Bryanite Socialism, explaining that without it, New York voters might get angry and adopt public ownership of streetcar lines and other franchises.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997378.E2.80.9379_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997378.E2.80.9379-85"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The New York state government affected many interests, and the power to make appointments to policy-making positions was a key role for the governor. Platt insisted that he be consulted; Roosevelt appeared to comply, but then made his own decisions. Historians marvel that Roosevelt managed to appoint so many first-rate men with Platt's approval. He even enlisted Platt's help in securing reform, such as in the spring of 1899, when Platt pressured state senators to vote for a civil service bill that the secretary of the Civil Service Reform Association called "superior to any civil service statute heretofore secured in America".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChessman196579_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChessman196579-86"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Chessman argues that as governor, Roosevelt developed the principles that shaped his presidency, especially insistence upon the public responsibility of large corporations, publicity as a first remedy for trusts, regulation of railroad rates, mediation of the conflict of capital and labor, conservation of natural resources and protection of the less fortunate members of society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChessman19656_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChessman19656-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_President">Vice President</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">United States presidential election, 1900</a></div>
<p>In November 1899, <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>'s first Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Garret_Hobart" title="Garret Hobart">Garret Hobart</a> died of heart failure. Theodore Roosevelt had anticipated a second term as governor or, alternatively, a cabinet post in the War Department; his friends (especially <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a>) saw this as a dead end. They supported him for Vice President, and no one else of prominence was actively seeking that job. Some people in the GOP wanted Roosevelt as Vice President. His friends were pushing, and so were his foes. Roosevelt's reforming zeal ran afoul of the insurance and franchise businesses, who had a major voice in the New York GOP. Platt engineered Roosevelt's removal from the state by pressuring him to accept the GOP nomination as Vice President in 1900. McKinley refused to consider Roosevelt as Secretary of War, but saw no risk in making him Vice President. Roosevelt accepted the nomination, although his campaign manager, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hanna" title="Mark Hanna">Mark Hanna</a>, thought Roosevelt was too cowboy-like. While the party executives were pleased with their success in engineering Roosevelt's next political foray, Roosevelt, very much to the contrary, thought he had "stood the state machine on its head".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997398_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997398-87"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The office of Vice President was a powerless sinecure, and did not suit Roosevelt's aggressive temperament.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> However, campaigning for the position added to his skills. Roosevelt threw himself into the campaign with his accustomed energy, crisscrossing the nation denouncing the radicalism of <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>, in contrast to the heroism of the soldiers and sailors who fought and won the war against Spain. Bryan had strongly supported the war itself, but he denounced the annexation of the Philippines as imperialism, which would spoil America's innocence. Roosevelt countered that it was best for the Filipinos to have stability, and the Americans to have a proud place in the world. With the nation basking in peace and prosperity, the voters gave McKinley <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1896" title="United States presidential election, 1896">an even larger landslide than in 1896.</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997388.E2.80.93405_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997388.E2.80.93405-89"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's six months as Vice President (March to September 1901) were uneventful with one singular exception:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997ch_14.E2.80.9315_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997ch_14.E2.80.9315-90"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 2, 1901, Roosevelt first publicized an aphorism that thrilled his supporters: "<a href="/wiki/Big_Stick_ideology" title="Big Stick ideology">Speak softly and carry a big stick, and you will go far."</a><sup id="cite_ref-Kelley_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley-91"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281901.E2.80.931909.29">Presidency (1901–1909)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -52px; left: -46px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:ROOSEVELT,_Theodore-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Roosevelt as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ROOSEVELT%2C_Theodore-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-ROOSEVELT%2C_Theodore-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="399" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ROOSEVELT%2C_Theodore-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-ROOSEVELT%2C_Theodore-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ROOSEVELT%2C_Theodore-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-ROOSEVELT%2C_Theodore-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8168" data-file-height="10025" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ROOSEVELT,_Theodore-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:ROOSEVELT, Theodore-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:ROOSEVELT, Theodore-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Roosevelt as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Presidency of Theodore Roosevelt">Presidency of Theodore Roosevelt</a></div>
<p>On September 6, President McKinley was shot by an anarchist acting alone while in Buffalo, New York. Initial reports suggested that his condition was improving, so Roosevelt, after visiting the ailing president, embarked for the west. When McKinley's condition worsened, Roosevelt rushed back. McKinley died on September 14, and Roosevelt was sworn in at the <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Inaugural_National_Historic_Site" title="Theodore Roosevelt Inaugural National Historic Site">Ansley Wilcox House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> The following month, Roosevelt invited <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> to dinner at the White House. To his dismay, this sparked a bitter, and at times vicious, reaction across the heavily <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">segregated</a> South. Roosevelt reacted with astonishment and protest, saying that he looked forward to many future dinners with Washington. Upon further reflection, Roosevelt wanted to ensure that this had no effect on political support in the South, and further invitations to Washington were avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997422.E2.80.9323_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997422.E2.80.9323-92"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt kept McKinley's Cabinet and promised to continue McKinley's policies. In the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1904" title="U.S. presidential election, 1904" class="mw-redirect">November 1904 presidential election</a>, Roosevelt won the presidency in his own right in a landslide victory against <a href="/wiki/Alton_B._Parker" title="Alton B. Parker">Alton Brooks Parker</a>. His vice president was <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Fairbanks" title="Charles W. Fairbanks">Charles Warren Fairbanks</a> of Indiana.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies">Domestic policies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent,_1903.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent%2C_1903.jpg/175px-Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent%2C_1903.jpg" width="175" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent%2C_1903.jpg/263px-Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent%2C_1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent%2C_1903.jpg/350px-Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent%2C_1903.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1997" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Roosevelt_by_John_Singer_Sargent,_1903.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Official White House portrait by John Singer Sargent</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trust_busting">Trust busting</span></h4>
<p>One of Roosevelt's first notable acts as president was to deliver a 20,000-word address to Congress<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> asking it to curb the power of large corporations (called "trusts"). He also spoke in support of organized labor to further chagrin big business, but to their delight, he endorsed the gold standard, protective tariffs and lower taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997428_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997428-94"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> For his aggressive use of <a href="/wiki/United_States_antitrust_law" title="United States antitrust law">United States antitrust law</a>, he became known as the "trust-buster". He brought 40 antitrust suits, and broke up major companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Northern_Securities_Company" title="Northern Securities Company">the largest railroad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a>, the largest oil company.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_strike">Coal strike</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_strike_of_1902" title="Coal strike of 1902">Coal strike of 1902</a></div>
<p>In May 1902, <a href="/wiki/United_Mine_Workers" title="United Mine Workers">anthracite coal miners</a> went on strike, threatening a national energy shortage. After threatening the coal operators with intervention by federal troops, Roosevelt won their agreement to an arbitration of the dispute by a commission, which succeeded in stopping the strike, dropping coal prices and retiring furnaces; the accord with <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan" title="J.P. Morgan" class="mw-redirect">J.P. Morgan</a> resulted in the workers getting more pay for fewer hours, but with no union recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997450.E2.80.9383_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997450.E2.80.9383-97"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Journalist Ray Baker quoted Roosevelt concerning his policy towards capitalists and laborers: "My action on labor should always be considered in connection with my action as regards capital, and both are reducible to my favorite formula—a square deal for every man."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997509_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997509-98"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt thought it was particularly important for the government to supervise the workings of the railway to avoid corruption in interstate commerce; the result was the Hepburn bill, which established control over railroad rates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997544.E2.80.9348_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997544.E2.80.9348-99"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pure_Food_and_drugs">Pure Food and drugs</span></h4>
