<p>Roosevelt responded to public anger over the abuses in the food packing industry by pushing Congress to pass the <a href="/wiki/Meat_Inspection_Act" title="Meat Inspection Act" class="mw-redirect">Meat Inspection Act</a> of 1906 and the <a href="/wiki/Pure_Food_and_Drug_Act" title="Pure Food and Drug Act">Pure Food and Drug Act</a>. The Meat Inspection Act of 1906 banned misleading labels and preservatives that contained harmful chemicals. The Pure Food and Drug Act banned food and drugs that were impure or falsely labeled from being made, sold, and shipped. Roosevelt also served as honorary president of the <a href="/wiki/American_School_Hygiene_Association" title="American School Hygiene Association">American School Hygiene Association</a> from 1907 to 1908, and in 1909 he convened the first <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> Conference on the Care of Dependent Children.<sup id="cite_ref-ProgHealth21_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ProgHealth21-100"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Business">Business</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg/200px-Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg/300px-Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg/400px-Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="628" data-file-height="471" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt-Fairbanks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt and Fairbanks, 1904</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1907" title="Panic of 1907">Panic of 1907</a>, nearly all agreed that a more flexible system to ensure liquidity was needed—the Republicans sought a response to the money supply through the bankers, whereas the Democrats sought government control; Roosevelt was unsure, but leaned towards the Republican view while continuing to denounce corporate corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997617.E2.80.9319_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997617.E2.80.9319-101"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, in 1910, Roosevelt commented on "enormously wealthy and economically powerful men" and suggested "a graduated inheritance tax on big fortunes... increasing rapidly in amount with the size of the estate".<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20140327_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140327-102"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WH-20111206_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH-20111206-103"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt was inclined to extend the regulatory reach of his office. In a moment of frustration, House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Gurney_Cannon" title="Joseph Gurney Cannon">Joseph Gurney Cannon</a> commented on Roosevelt's desire for executive branch control in domestic policy-making: "That fellow at the other end of the avenue wants everything from the birth of Christ to the death of the devil." Biographer Brands states that, "Even his friends occasionally wondered whether there wasn't any custom or practice too minor for him to try to regulate, update or otherwise improve."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997552.E2.80.9353_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997552.E2.80.9353-104"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> In fact, Roosevelt's willingness to exercise his power included attempted rule changes in the game of football; at the Naval Academy, he sought to force retention of martial arts classes and to revise disciplinary rules. He even ordered changes made in the minting of a coin whose design he disliked, and ordered the Government Printing Office to adopt simplified spellings for a core list of 300 words, according to reformers on the <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Spelling_Board" title="Simplified Spelling Board">Simplified Spelling Board</a>. He was forced to rescind the latter after substantial ridicule from the press and a resolution of protest from the House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997553.E2.80.9356_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997553.E2.80.9356-105"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Theodore_Roosevelt#Conservation" title="Presidency of Theodore Roosevelt">Presidency of Theodore Roosevelt § Conservation</a></div>
<p>Of all Roosevelt's achievements, he was proudest of his work in conservation of natural resources, and extending Federal protection to land and wildlife. Roosevelt established the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">United States Forest Service</a>, signed into law the creation of five <a href="/wiki/National_parks_(United_States)" title="National parks (United States)" class="mw-redirect">National Parks</a>, and signed the 1906 <a href="/wiki/Antiquities_Act" title="Antiquities Act">Antiquities Act</a>, under which he proclaimed 18 new <a href="/wiki/U.S._National_Monument" title="U.S. National Monument" class="mw-redirect">U.S. National Monuments</a>. He also established the first 51 <a href="/wiki/Bird_Reserve" title="Bird Reserve" class="mw-redirect">Bird Reserves</a>, four <a href="/w/index.php?title=Game_Preserve&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Game Preserve (page does not exist)">Game Preserves</a>, and 150 <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Forest" title="United States National Forest">National Forests</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Shoshone_National_Forest" title="Shoshone National Forest">Shoshone National Forest</a>, the nation's first. The area of the United States that he placed under public protection totals approximately 230,000,000 acres (930,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). He worked closely with <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg/175px-Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg" width="175" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg/263px-Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg/350px-Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="390" data-file-height="429" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_monroe_Doctrine_cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt Corollary of Monroe Doctrine depicted</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the late 1890s, Roosevelt had been an ardent imperialist, and vigorously defended the permanent acquisition of the Philippines in the 1900 election campaign. After the rebellion ended in 1901, he largely lost interest in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and Asian expansion in general, despite the contradictory opinion of his Secretary of War, William Howard Taft. As president, he primarily focused the nation's overseas ambitions on the Caribbean, especially locations that had a bearing on the defense of his pet project, the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997615.E2.80.9316_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997615.E2.80.9316-107"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1905, Roosevelt offered to mediate a treaty to end the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a>. The parties agreed to meet in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_New_Hampshire" title="Portsmouth, New Hampshire">Portsmouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> and they resolved the final conflict over the division of <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a>– <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> took the northern half, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> the south; Japan also dropped its demand for an indemnity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997530.E2.80.9339_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997530.E2.80.9339-108"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for his successful efforts. <a href="/wiki/George_E._Mowry" title="George E. Mowry">George E. Mowry</a> concludes that Roosevelt handled the arbitration well, doing an "excellent job of balancing Russian and Japanese power in the Orient, where the supremacy of either constituted a threat to growing America".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gentlemen%27s_Agreement_of_1907" title="Gentlemen's Agreement of 1907">Gentlemen's Agreement of 1907</a> resolved unpleasant racial tensions with Japan. <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> was angered over the segregation of Japanese children in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> schools. The tensions were ended, but Japan also agreed not to allow unskilled workers to emigrate to the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGould2011249.E2.80.9352_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2011249.E2.80.9352-111"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span></h4>
<p>Roosevelt's attention concerning Latin American turmoil was heightened by his plans for building a canal. In December 1902, the <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germans</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">English</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italians</a> sought to impose a naval blockade against <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> in order to force the repayment of delinquent loans. Roosevelt was particularly concerned with the motives of Germany's <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Kaiser Wilhelm</a>. He succeeded in getting the aggressors to agree to arbitration by a tribunal at <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>, and averted the <a href="/wiki/Venezuela_Crisis_of_1902%E2%80%931903" title="Venezuela Crisis of 1902–1903" class="mw-redirect">Venezuela Crisis of 1902–1903</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997464_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997464-112"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> The latitude granted to the Europeans by the arbiters was in part responsible for the "<a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Corollary" title="Roosevelt Corollary">Roosevelt Corollary</a>" to the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>, which the President issued in 1904: "Chronic wrongdoing or an impotence which results in a general loosening of the ties of civilized society, may in America, as elsewhere, ultimately require intervention by some civilized nation, and in the Western Hemisphere, the adherence of the United States to the Monroe doctrine may force the United States, however reluctantly, in flagrant cases of such wrongdoing or impotence, to the exercise of an international police power."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997527_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997527-113"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The pursuit of an <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Panama_Canal" title="History of the Panama Canal">isthmus canal</a> in Central America during this period focused on two possible routes—<a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, which was then a rebellious district within <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>. Roosevelt convinced Congress to approve the Panamanian alternative, and a treaty was approved, only to be rejected by the Colombian government. When the Panamanians learned of this, a rebellion followed, was supported by Roosevelt, and succeeded. A <a href="/wiki/Hay%E2%80%93Bunau-Varilla_Treaty" title="Hay–Bunau-Varilla Treaty">treaty</a> with the new Panama government for construction of the canal was then reached in 1903. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997482.E2.80.9386_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997482.E2.80.9386-114"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1906, following a disputed election, an insurrection ensued in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>; Roosevelt sent Taft, the Secretary of War, to monitor the situation; he was convinced that he had the authority to unilaterally authorize Taft to deploy Marines if necessary, without congressional approval.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997570_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997570-115"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Examining the work of numerous scholars, Ricard (2014) reports that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The most striking evolution in the twenty-first century historiography of Theodore Roosevelt is the switch from a partial arraignment of the imperialist to a quasi-unanimous celebration of the master diplomatist.... [Regarding British relations these studies] have underlined cogently Roosevelt's exceptional statesmanship in the construction of the nascent twentieth-century "special relationship". ...The twenty-sixth president's reputation as a brilliant diplomatist and realpolitician has undeniably reached new heights in the twenty-first century...yet, his Philippine policy still prompts criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_media">The media</span></h3>
<p>Building on McKinley's effective use of the press, Roosevelt made the White House the center of news every day, providing interviews and photo opportunities. After noticing the reporters huddled outside the White House in the rain one day, he gave them their own room inside, effectively inventing the presidential press briefing. The grateful press, with unprecedented access to the White House, rewarded Roosevelt with ample coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-117"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt normally enjoyed very close relationships with the press, which he used to keep in daily contact with his middle-class base. While out of office, he made a living as a writer and magazine editor. He loved talking with intellectuals, authors, and writers. He drew the line, however, at expose-oriented scandal-mongering journalists who, during his term, set magazine subscriptions soaring by their attacks on corrupt politicians, mayors, and corporations. Roosevelt himself was not usually a target, but his speech in 1906 coined the term "<a href="/wiki/Muckraker" title="Muckraker">muckraker</a>" for unscrupulous journalists making wild charges. "The liar," he said, "is no whit better than the thief, and if his mendacity takes the form of slander he may be worse than most thieves."<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The press did briefly target Roosevelt in one instance. Ever since 1904, he had been periodically criticized for the manner in which he facilitated the Panama Canal. In the least judicious use of executive power, according to biographer Brands, Roosevelt, near the end of his term, demanded that the Justice Department bring charges of criminal libel against Joseph Pulitzer's <i>New York World</i>. The publication had accused him of "deliberate misstatements of fact" in defense of family members who were criticized as a result of the Panama affair. Though indictment was obtained, the case was ultimately dismissed in federal court—it was not a federal offense, but one enforceable at the state court level. The Justice Department had predicted that result, and had also advised Roosevelt accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997633.E2.80.9334_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997633.E2.80.9334-119"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1904">Election of 1904</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1904.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ElectoralCollege1904.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1904.svg.png" width="300" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ElectoralCollege1904.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1904.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ElectoralCollege1904.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1904.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1003" data-file-height="572" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1904.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1904 election results</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1904" title="United States presidential election, 1904">United States presidential election, 1904</a></div>
<p>The control and management of the Republican Party lay in the hands of chairman Mark Hanna until McKinley's death. Hanna's domination, and potential rivalry for the party's nomination in 1904, began to wane with his own health issues and ultimate death early that year. In deference to Hanna's conservative loyalists, Roosevelt at first offered the party chairmanship to <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Bliss" title="Cornelius Bliss" class="mw-redirect">Cornelius Bliss</a>, but he declined. Roosevelt then turned to his own man, <a href="/wiki/George_B._Cortelyou" title="George B. Cortelyou">George B. Cortelyou</a> of New York, the first Secretary of Commerce and Labor. To buttress his hold on the party's nomination, Roosevelt made it clear that anyone opposing Cortelyou would be considered to be opposing the President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997501.E2.80.933_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997501.E2.80.933-120"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> The President secured his own nomination, but his preferred vice-presidential running mate, <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Hitt" title="Robert R. Hitt">Robert R. Hitt</a>, wasn't nominated. <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Fairbanks" title="Charles W. Fairbanks">Charles Warren Fairbanks</a> was nominated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997504_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997504-121"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While Roosevelt followed the tradition of incumbents in not actively campaigning on the stump, he sought to control the campaign's message through specific instructions to Cortelyou. He also attempted to manage the press's release of White House statements by forming the Ananias Club. Any journalist who repeated a statement made by the president without approval was penalized by restriction of further access.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997507_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997507-122"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Democratic Party's nominee in 1904 was <a href="/wiki/Alton_B._Parker" title="Alton B. Parker">Alton Brooks Parker</a>. Roosevelt won 56% of the popular vote, and Parker received 38%; Roosevelt also won the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> vote, 336 to 140. Before his inauguration ceremony, Roosevelt declared that he would not serve another term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997513.E2.80.9314_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997513.E2.80.9314-123"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1908">Election of 1908</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1908" title="United States presidential election, 1908">United States presidential election, 1908</a></div>
<p>Before leaving office, while attempting to push through the nomination of <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> for the Presidency in <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1908" title="U.S. presidential election, 1908" class="mw-redirect">1908</a>, Roosevelt declared Taft to be a "genuine progressive". In January of that year, Roosevelt wrote the following to Taft: "Dear Will: Do you want any action about those federal officials? I will break their necks with the utmost cheerfulness if you say the word!" Just weeks later he branded as "false and malicious"; the charge was that he was using the offices at his disposal to favor Taft.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997626_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997626-124"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taft easily defeated three-time candidate William Jennings Bryan. Taft promoted a different progressivism, one that stressed the rule of law and preferred that judges rather than administrators or politicians make the basic decisions about fairness. Taft usually proved to be a less adroit politician than Roosevelt and lacked the energy and personal magnetism, along with the publicity devices, the dedicated supporters, and the broad base of public support that made Roosevelt so formidable. When Roosevelt realized that lowering the tariff would risk creating severe tensions inside the Republican Party by pitting producers (manufacturers and farmers) against merchants and consumers, he stopped talking about the issue. Taft ignored the risks and tackled the tariff boldly, encouraging reformers to fight for lower rates, and then cutting deals with conservative leaders that kept overall rates high. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Payne-Aldrich_tariff" title="Payne-Aldrich tariff" class="mw-redirect">Payne-Aldrich tariff</a> of 1909 was too high for most reformers, but instead of blaming this on Senator <a href="/wiki/Nelson_W._Aldrich" title="Nelson W. Aldrich">Nelson Wilmarth Aldrich</a> and big businesses, Taft took credit, calling it the best tariff ever. He again had managed to alienate all sides. While the crisis was building inside the Party, Roosevelt was touring Africa and Europe, to allow Taft to be his own man.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_Party_schism">Republican Party schism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg/175px-For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg" width="175" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg/263px-For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg/350px-For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1109" data-file-height="1421" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:For_Auld_Lang_Syne_-_Leonard_Raven-Hill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Punch</i> depicts GOP Schism between Taft and TR</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt had attempted to refashion Taft into a younger version of himself, but as soon as Taft began to display his individuality, Roosevelt unveiled his disenchantment. He was even offended on election night when Taft wrote and indicated that his success had been possible not just through the efforts of Roosevelt, but also his brother Charley. Roosevelt was further alienated when Taft, again intent on becoming his own man, did not consult him about cabinet appointments. Lodge empathized with Roosevelt, and therefore declined an offer to become Secretary of State.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997665.E2.80.9366_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997665.E2.80.9366-126"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike Roosevelt, Taft never attacked business or businessmen in his rhetoric. However, he was attentive to the law, so he launched 90 antitrust suits, including one against the largest corporation, US Steel, for an acquisition that Roosevelt had personally approved. Consequently, Taft lost the support of antitrust reformers (who disliked his conservative rhetoric), of big business (which disliked his actions), and of Roosevelt, who felt humiliated by his protégé. More trouble came when Taft fired Roosevelt's friend and appointee <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a>, a leading conservationist. Pinchot alleged that Taft's Secretary of the Interior, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ballinger" title="Richard Ballinger" class="mw-redirect">Richard Ballinger</a>, was in league with big timber interests. Conservationists sided with Pinchot, and Taft alienated yet another vocal constituency. The left wing of the Republican Party began turning against Taft. Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._La_Follette_Sr." title="Robert M. La Follette Sr.">Robert M. La Follette Sr.</a> of Wisconsin joined with Pinchot, William White and Hiram Johnson to create the National Progressive Republican League; their objectives were to defeat the power of political bossism at the state level and to replace Taft at the national level. Roosevelt declined to join this group—he was still reluctant to leave the GOP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997683_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997683-127"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt, back from Europe, unexpectedly launched an attack on the courts. He gave his famous speech at Osawatomie, Kansas, in August 1910, which was the most radical of his career and openly initiated his break with the Taft administration and the conservative Republicans. Osawatomie was well known as the base used by <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(abolitionist)" title="John Brown (abolitionist)">John Brown</a> when he launched his bloody attacks on slavery. Advocating a program of "New Nationalism", Roosevelt emphasized the priority of labor over capital interests, a need to more effectively control corporate creation and combination, and proposed a ban on corporate political contributions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997675_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997675-128"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt shortly thereafter made it clear in a meeting with <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Carpenter_Griscom" title="Lloyd Carpenter Griscom">Lloyd Carpenter Griscom</a>, a New York Republican regular, that Taft no longer enjoyed his support, since he had "deliberately abandoned" their previous close relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997677_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997677-129"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Taft was deeply upset. In the 1910 Congressional elections, Democrats won a majority in the House, and significantly diminished the Republican's hold on the Senate; Taft's reelection in 1912 was doubtful. The Republican progressives interpreted the defeat as compelling argument for the complete reorganization of the party in 1911, and Roosevelt reacted with renewed interest in more personal political endeavors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997683_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997683-127"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite skepticism of La Follette's new League, Roosevelt expressed general support for progressive principles; in fact, between January and April 1911, Roosevelt wrote a series of articles for <i>The Outlook</i>, defending what he called "the great movement of our day, the progressive nationalist movement against special privilege, and in favor of an honest and efficient political and industrial democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997684_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997684-130"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smithsonian-Roosevelt_African_Expedition_.281909.E2.80.9310.29">Smithsonian-Roosevelt African Expedition (1909–10)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg/220px-Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg/330px-Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg/440px-Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3647" data-file-height="2631" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_safari_elephant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt standing next to the elephant he shot on safari</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 1909, shortly after the end of his presidency, Roosevelt left New York for the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian-Roosevelt_African_Expedition" title="Smithsonian-Roosevelt African Expedition" class="mw-redirect">Smithsonian-Roosevelt African Expedition</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Safari" title="Safari">safari</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">central Africa</a> outfitted by the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Science1908_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science1908-131"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's party landed in <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" title="British East Africa" class="mw-redirect">British East Africa</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>), traveled to the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>), before following the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> to <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>. Financed by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a> and by his own writings, Roosevelt's party hunted for specimens for the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> and for the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-SIA_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIA-132"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> The group, led by the legendary hunter-tracker RJ Cunninghame, included scientists from the Smithsonian, and was joined from time to time by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Selous" title="Frederick Selous">Frederick Selous</a>, the famous big game hunter and explorer. Among other items, Roosevelt brought with him four tons of salt for preserving animal hides, a lucky rabbit's foot given to him by boxer <a href="/wiki/John_L._Sullivan" title="John L. Sullivan">John L. Sullivan</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Holland_%26_Holland" title="Holland &amp; Holland">Holland &amp; Holland</a> <a href="/wiki/Double_rifle" title="Double rifle">double rifle</a> in <a href="/wiki/.500/450_Nitro_Express" title=".500/450 Nitro Express">.500/450</a> donated by a group of 56 admiring Britons, a <a href="/wiki/Winchester_1895" title="Winchester 1895" class="mw-redirect">Winchester 1895</a> rifle in <a href="/wiki/.405_Winchester" title=".405 Winchester">.405 Winchester</a>, an Army (<a href="/wiki/M1903_Springfield" title="M1903 Springfield">M1903</a>) Springfield in <a href="/wiki/.30-06_Springfield" title=".30-06 Springfield">.30-06</a> caliber stocked and sighted for him, a Fox No. 12 shotgun, and the famous Pigskin Library, a collection of classics bound in pig leather and transported in a single reinforced trunk. Participants on the expedition included <a href="/wiki/Kermit_Roosevelt" title="Kermit Roosevelt">Kermit Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Alexander_Mearns" title="Edgar Alexander Mearns">Edgar Alexander Mearns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Heller" title="Edmund Heller">Edmund Heller</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Alden_Loring" title="John Alden Loring">John Alden Loring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt and his companions killed or trapped approximately 11,400<sup id="cite_ref-SIA_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIA-132"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> animals, from insects and moles to hippopotamuses and elephants. The 1000 large animals included 512 <a href="/wiki/Big-game_hunting" title="Big-game hunting">big game</a> animals, including six rare <a href="/wiki/White_rhinoceros" title="White rhinoceros">White rhinos</a>. Tons of salted animals and their skins were shipped to Washington; it took years to mount them all, and the Smithsonian shared many duplicate specimens with other museums. Regarding the large number of animals taken, Roosevelt said, "I can be condemned only if the existence of the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, and all similar zoological institutions are to be condemned".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Toole200567_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Toole200567-134"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although the safari was ostensibly conducted in the name of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, it was as much a political and social event as it was a hunting excursion; Roosevelt interacted with renowned professional hunters and land-owning families, and met many native peoples and local leaders. Roosevelt became a life member of the <a href="/wiki/National_Rifle_Association" title="National Rifle Association">National Rifle Association</a> in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> He wrote a detailed account of the safari in the book <i>African Game Trails</i>, recounting the excitement of the chase, the people he met, and the flora and fauna he collected in the name of science.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1912">Election of 1912</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1912" title="U.S. presidential election, 1912" class="mw-redirect">U.S. presidential election, 1912</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive Party (United States, 1912)</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_primaries_and_convention">Republican primaries and convention</span></h4>
<p>In November 1911, a group of Ohio Republicans endorsed Roosevelt for the party's nomination for president; the endorsers included <a href="/wiki/James_R._Garfield" title="James R. Garfield" class="mw-redirect">James R. Garfield</a> and Dan Hanna. This was notable, in that it emanated from Taft's home state. Roosevelt conspicuously declined to make a statement requested by Garfield—that he flatly refuse a nomination. Soon thereafter, Roosevelt said, "I am really sorry for Taft... I am sure he means well, but he means well feebly, and he does not know how! He is utterly unfit for leadership and this is a time when we need leadership." In January 1912, Roosevelt declared "if the people make a draft on me I shall not decline to serve".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997698_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997698-137"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year Roosevelt spoke before the Constitutional Convention in Ohio, openly referred to himself as a progressive and endorsed progressive reforms—even endorsing popular review of state judicial decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997703_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997703-138"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> In reaction to Roosevelt's proposals for popular overrule of court decisions, Taft said, "such extremists are not progressives—they are political emotionalists or neurotics".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997709_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997709-139"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt began to envision himself as the savior of the Republican party (the "GOP") from defeat in the upcoming Presidential election and announced himself as a candidate for the GOP banner. In February 1912, Roosevelt announced in Boston "I will accept the nomination for president if it is tendered to me. I hope that so far as possible the people may be given the chance through direct primaries to express who shall be the nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997705_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997705-140"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gloriousr_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gloriousr-141"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Both Elihu Root and Cabot Lodge could then see the ultimate defeat of the party in the next election due to this lethal division; Taft considered that he was witnessing the end of his career—it was only a matter of whether he would be defeated by his own party or in the general election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997706_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997706-142"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 1912 primaries represented the first extensive use of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_primary" title="United States presidential primary">presidential primary</a>, a reform achievement of the progressive movement. The primaries in the South, where party regulars dominated, went for Taft, as well as New York, Indiana, Michigan, Kentucky and Massachusetts. Meanwhile, Roosevelt won in Illinois, Minnesota, Nebraska, South Dakota, California, Maryland and Pennsylvania; Roosevelt also won Taft's home state of Ohio. These primary elections, while demonstrating Roosevelt's continuing popularity with the electorate, were not pivotal. The final credentials of the state delegates at the national convention were determined by the national committee, which was controlled by the party leaders, headed by the incumbent president. At the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Convention" title="Republican Convention" class="mw-redirect">Republican Convention</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, though Taft's victory was not immediate, the ultimate outcome was inevitably in his favor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997712_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997712-143"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_the_Progressive_.28.22Bull_Moose.22.29_Party">Formation of the Progressive ("Bull Moose") Party</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Nationalism" title="New Nationalism">New Nationalism</a></div>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">

</div>
</td>
