<p>Once his defeat as the GOP nominee was probable, Roosevelt announced that he would "accept the progressive nomination on a progressive platform and I shall fight to the end, win or lose". At the same time, Roosevelt prophetically said, "My feeling is that the Democrats will probably win if they nominate a progressive".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997717_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997717-144"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> After two weeks at the GOP convention, Roosevelt asked his followers to leave the hall, and they moved to the <a href="/wiki/Auditorium_Building,_Chicago" title="Auditorium Building, Chicago">Auditorium Theatre</a>. Then Roosevelt, along with key allies such as <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Pinchot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Beveridge" title="Albert Beveridge" class="mw-redirect">Albert Beveridge</a> created the Progressive Party, structuring it as a permanent organization that would field complete tickets at the presidential and state level. It was popularly known as the "Bull Moose Party", which got its name after Roosevelt told reporters, "I'm as fit as a bull moose".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> At the convention Roosevelt cried out, "We stand at <a href="/wiki/Armageddon" title="Armageddon">Armageddon</a> and we battle for the Lord." Roosevelt's platform echoed his 1907–8 proposals, calling for vigorous government intervention to protect the people from the selfish interests;</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>To destroy this invisible Government, to dissolve the <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/unholy_alliance" class="extiw" title="wikt:unholy alliance">unholy alliance</a> between corrupt business and corrupt politics is the first task of the statesmanship of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1913.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.bartleby.com.2F55.2F15b.html_XV._The_Peace_of_Righteousness.2C_Appendix_B.5D_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1913.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.bartleby.com.2F55.2F15b.html_XV._The_Peace_of_Righteousness.2C_Appendix_B.5D-147"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> This country belongs to the people. Its resources, its business, its laws, its institutions, should be utilized, maintained, or altered in whatever manner will best promote the general interest. This assertion is explicit... Mr. Wilson must know that every monopoly in the United States opposes the Progressive party... I challenge him... to name the monopoly that did support the Progressive party, whether... the Sugar Trust, the US Steel Trust, the Harvester Trust, the Standard Oil Trust, the Tobacco Trust, or any other... Ours was the only program to which they objected, and they supported either Mr. Wilson or Mr. Taft<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1919Chapter_XXII.2C_pp._25.E2.80.9331_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1919Chapter_XXII.2C_pp._25.E2.80.9331-148"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination_attempt">Assassination attempt</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg/220px-TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg/330px-TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg/440px-TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="750" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TR_Assissination_Bullet_Damage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The bullet-damaged speech and eyeglass case on display at the Theodore Roosevelt Birthplace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On October 14, 1912, while campaigning in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, Roosevelt was shot by a saloonkeeper named <a href="/wiki/John_Flammang_Schrank" title="John Flammang Schrank">John Flammang Schrank</a>. The bullet lodged in his chest after penetrating his steel eyeglass case and passing through a thick (50 pages) single-folded copy of the speech he was carrying in his jacket.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt, as an experienced hunter and anatomist, correctly concluded that since he was not coughing blood, the bullet had not reached his lung, and he declined suggestions to go to the hospital immediately. Instead, he delivered his <a href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/I_have_just_been_shot" class="extiw" title="s:I have just been shot">scheduled speech</a> with blood seeping into his shirt.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> He spoke for 90 minutes. His opening comments to the gathered crowd were, "Ladies and gentlemen, I don't know whether you fully understand that I have just been shot, but it takes more than that to kill a Bull Moose."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Afterwards, probes and an x-ray showed that the bullet had lodged in Roosevelt's chest muscle, but did not penetrate the <a href="/wiki/Pleura" title="Pleura" class="mw-redirect">pleura</a>, and it would be less dangerous to leave it in place. Roosevelt carried the bullet with him for the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Because of the bullet wound, Roosevelt was taken off the campaign trail in the final weeks of the race (which ended on election day, November 5). Though the other two campaigners stopped their own campaigns during the week Roosevelt was in the hospital, they resumed it once he was released. The bullet lodged in his chest exacerbated his <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">rheumatoid arthritis</a> and prevented him from doing his daily stint of exercises; Roosevelt would soon become obese.<sup id="cite_ref-oimjjac_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oimjjac-153"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> In an era of party loyalty, Roosevelt failed to move enough Republicans to vote a third party ticket. He did win 4.1 million votes (27%), compared to Taft's 3.5 million (23%). However, Wilson's 6.3 million votes (42%) were enough to garner 435 electoral votes. Roosevelt had 88 electoral votes, and Taft had 8. Pennsylvania was Roosevelt's only eastern state; in the Midwest, he carried Michigan, Minnesota, and South Dakota; in the West, California, and Washington; he did not win any southern states.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1913.E2.80.9314_South_American_Expedition">1913–14 South American Expedition</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt%E2%80%93Rondon_Scientific_Expedition" title="Roosevelt–Rondon Scientific Expedition">Roosevelt–Rondon Scientific Expedition</a></div>
<p>A friend of Roosevelt's, Father <a href="/wiki/John_Augustine_Zahm" title="John Augustine Zahm">John Augustine Zahm</a>, a Catholic priest and scientist at the University of Notre Dame, had searched for new adventures and found them in the forests of South America. After a briefing of several of his own expeditions, he persuaded Roosevelt to participate in such an expedition in 1912. To finance the expedition, Roosevelt received support from the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, promising to bring back many new animal specimens. Roosevelt's popular book, <i>Through the Brazilian Wilderness</i><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> describes his expedition into the Brazilian jungle in 1913 as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt-Rondon_Scientific_Expedition" title="Roosevelt-Rondon Scientific Expedition" class="mw-redirect">Roosevelt-Rondon Scientific Expedition</a>, co-named after its leader, Brazilian explorer <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A2ndido_Rondon" title="Cândido Rondon">Cândido Rondon</a>. The book describes the scientific discovery, scenic tropical vistas, and exotic flora and fauna experienced during the adventure.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:River-doubt-team.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/River-doubt-team.jpg/220px-River-doubt-team.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/River-doubt-team.jpg/330px-River-doubt-team.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/River-doubt-team.jpg/440px-River-doubt-team.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1039" data-file-height="694" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River-doubt-team.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From left to right (seated): Fr. John Augustine Zahm, Cândido Rondon, Kermit Roosevelt, Cherrie, Miller, four Brazilians, Roosevelt, Fiala. Only Roosevelt, Kermit, Cherrie, Rondon, and the Brazilians traveled down the River of Doubt.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once in South America, a new, far more ambitious goal was added: to find the headwaters of the Rio da Duvida, the <a href="/wiki/River_of_Doubt" title="River of Doubt" class="mw-redirect">River of Doubt</a>, and trace it north to the Madeira and thence to the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a>. It was later renamed <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_River" title="Roosevelt River">Roosevelt River</a> in honor of the former President. Roosevelt's crew consisted of his son <a href="/wiki/Kermit_Roosevelt" title="Kermit Roosevelt">Kermit</a>, naturalist Colonel Rondon, George K. Cherrie, sent by the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, Brazilian Lieutenant João Lira, team physician Dr. José Antonio Cajazeira, and 16 skilled paddlers and porters (called <i>camaradas</i> [comrades] in <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>). The initial expedition started somewhat tenuously on December 9, 1913, at the height of the rainy season. The trip down the River of Doubt started on February 27, 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the trip down the river, Roosevelt suffered a minor leg wound after he jumped into the river to try to prevent two canoes from smashing against the rocks. The flesh wound he received, however, soon gave him <a href="/wiki/Tropical_fever" title="Tropical fever" class="mw-redirect">tropical fever</a> that resembled the <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> he had contracted while in Cuba fifteen years before.<sup id="cite_ref-oimjjac_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oimjjac-153"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Because the bullet lodged in his chest from the assassination attempt in 1912 was never removed, his health worsened from the infection.<sup id="cite_ref-DeathNews_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeathNews-157"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> This weakened Roosevelt so greatly that six weeks into the adventure, he had to be attended to day and night by the expedition's physician and his son Kermit. By then, he could not walk because of the infection in his injured leg and an infirmity in the other, which was due to a traffic accident a decade earlier. Roosevelt was riddled with chest pains, fighting a fever that soared to 103&#160;°F (39&#160;°C) and at times made him delirious. Regarding his condition as a threat to the survival of the others, Roosevelt insisted he be left behind to allow the poorly provisioned expedition to proceed as rapidly as it could. Only an appeal by his son persuaded him to continue.<sup id="cite_ref-Millard.2C_2009_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millard.2C_2009-158"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Roosevelt's continued decline and loss of over 50 pounds (20&#160;kg), Commander Rondon reduced the pace of the expedition to allow for his commission's mapmaking and other geographical tasks, which required regular stops to fix the expedition's position by sun-based survey. Upon Roosevelt's return to New York, friends and family were startled by his physical appearance and fatigue. Roosevelt wrote, perhaps prophetically, to a friend that the trip had cut his life short by ten years. For the rest of his few remaining years, he would be plagued by flare-ups of malaria and leg inflammations so severe as to require surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer19194.E2.80.937_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer19194.E2.80.937-159"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Before Roosevelt had even completed his sea voyage home, critics raised doubts over his claims of exploring and navigating a completely uncharted river over 625 miles (1,000&#160;km) long. When he had recovered sufficiently, he addressed a standing-room-only convention organized in Washington, D.C., by the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a> and satisfactorily defended his claims.<sup id="cite_ref-Millard.2C_2009_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millard.2C_2009-158"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg/175px-Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg" width="175" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg/263px-Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg/350px-Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2307" data-file-height="2743" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Roosevelt_Hotel_Allen_1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ex-President Theodore Roosevelt in Allentown, Pennsylvania, 1914.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_entry_into_World_War_I" title="American entry into World War I">American entry into World War I</a></div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt%27s_World_War_I_volunteers" title="Roosevelt's World War I volunteers">Roosevelt's World War I volunteers</a></div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> began in 1914, Roosevelt strongly supported the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a> and demanded a harsher policy against Germany, especially regarding submarine warfare. Roosevelt angrily denounced the foreign policy of President Wilson, calling it a failure regarding the atrocities in Belgium and the violations of American rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997749.E2.80.9351.2C_806.E2.80.939_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997749.E2.80.9351.2C_806.E2.80.939-160"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1916, he campaigned energetically for <a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles Evans Hughes</a> and repeatedly denounced Irish-Americans and German-Americans who Roosevelt said were unpatriotic, because they put the interests of Ireland and Germany ahead of America's by supporting neutrality. He insisted that one had to be 100% American, not a "<a href="/wiki/Hyphenated_American" title="Hyphenated American">hyphenated American</a>" who juggled multiple loyalties. In March 1917, Congress gave Roosevelt the authority to raise a maximum of four divisions similar to the Rough Riders, and Major <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Russell_Burnham" title="Frederick Russell Burnham">Frederick Russell Burnham</a> was put in charge of both the general organization and recruitment.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-1917-0313_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-1917-0313-162"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">Commander-in-chief</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, announced to the press that he would not send Roosevelt and his volunteers to France, but instead would send an American Expeditionary Force under the command of General <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">John J. Pershing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-1917-0517_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-1917-0517-163"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was left with no option except to disband the volunteers. He never forgave Wilson, and quickly published <i>The Foes Of Our Own Household</i>, an indictment of the sitting president.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997781.E2.80.934_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997781.E2.80.934-165"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt's attacks on Wilson helped the Republicans win control of Congress in the off-year elections of 1918. Roosevelt was popular enough to seriously contest the 1920 Republican nomination, but his health was broken by 1918, because of the lingering <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>. His family and supporters threw their support behind Roosevelt's old military companion, General <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Wood" title="Leonard Wood">Leonard Wood</a>, who was ultimately defeated by Taft supporter <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's youngest son, Quentin, a pilot with the American forces in France, was shot down behind German lines on July 14, 1918, at the age of 20. It is said that Quentin's death distressed Roosevelt so much that he never recovered from his loss.<sup id="cite_ref-Dalton.2C_2002_p._507_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalton.2C_2002_p._507-168"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<p>On the night of January 5, 1919, Roosevelt experienced breathing problems. He felt better after treatment from his physician, Dr. George W. Faller, and went to bed. Roosevelt's last words were "Please put out that light, James" to his family servant James Amos. Between 4:00 AM and 4:15 AM the next morning, Roosevelt died in his sleep at <a href="/wiki/Sagamore_Hill_(house)" title="Sagamore Hill (house)">Sagamore Hill</a> as a result of a blood clot detaching from a vein and traveling to his lungs.<sup id="cite_ref-DeathNews_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeathNews-157"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> Upon receiving word of his death, his son Archibald telegraphed his siblings simply, "The old lion is dead."<sup id="cite_ref-Dalton.2C_2002_p._507_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalton.2C_2002_p._507-168"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> Woodrow Wilson's vice president, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_R._Marshall" title="Thomas R. Marshall">Thomas R. Marshall</a>, said that "Death had to take Roosevelt sleeping, for if he had been awake, there would have been a fight."<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_positions_and_speeches">Political positions and speeches</span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" width="38" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original works written by or about:<br />
<b style="text-align: center;"><i><a href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/Author:Theodore_Roosevelt" class="extiw" title="s:Author:Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a></i></b></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Political positions of Theodore Roosevelt">Political positions of Theodore Roosevelt</a></div>
<p>Theodore Roosevelt introduced the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Square_Deal" title="Square Deal">Square Deal</a>" to describe his progressive views in a <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Political positions of Theodore Roosevelt">speech</a> delivered after leaving the office of the Presidency in August 1910. In his broad outline, he stressed equality of opportunity for all citizens and emphasized the importance of fair government regulations of corporate "special interests". Roosevelt was one of the first Presidents to make <a href="/wiki/Conservation_(ethic)" title="Conservation (ethic)">conservation</a> a national issue. In his speech at Osawatomie, Kansas, on August 31, 1910, he outlined his views on conservation of the lands of the United States. He favored using America's natural resources, but opposed wasteful consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-TR:_Conservation_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TR:_Conservation-170"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> One of his most lasting legacies was his significant role in the creation of 5 national parks, 18 national monuments, and 150 National Forests, among other works of conservation. Roosevelt was instrumental in conserving about 230&#160;million acres (930,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of American soil among various parks and other federal projects.<sup id="cite_ref-TRAssn_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRAssn-171"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 21st century, historians have paid renewed attention to President Roosevelt as "The Wilderness Warrior" and his energetic promotion of the conservation movement. He collaborated with his chief advisor, <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a>, the chief of the Forest Service. Pinchot and Roosevelt scheduled a series of news events that garnered nationwide media attention in magazines and newspapers. They used magazine articles, speeches, press conferences, interviews, and especially large-scale presidential commissions. Roosevelt's goal was to encourage his middle-class reform-minded base to add conservation to their list of issues.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positions_on_immigration.2C_minorities.2C_civil_rights.2C_and_eugenics">Positions on immigration, minorities, civil rights, and eugenics</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span></h4>
<p>In an 1894 article on immigration, Roosevelt said, "We must Americanize in every way, in speech, in political ideas and principles, and in their way of looking at relations between church and state. We welcome the German and the Irishman who becomes an American. We have no use for the German or Irishman who remains such... He must revere only our flag, not only must it come first, but no other flag should even come second."<sup id="cite_ref-.2770s_267_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.2770s_267-173"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt took an active interest in immigration, and had launched an extensive reorganization of the federal immigration depot at Ellis Island within months of assuming the presidency. Roosevelt "straddled the immigration question",<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> taking the position that "we cannot have too much immigration of the right sort, and we should have none whatever of the wrong sort".<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> As president, his stated preferences were relatively inclusive, across the then diverse and mostly European sources of immigration:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is unwise to depart from the old American tradition and discriminate for or against any man who desires to come here and become a citizen, save on the ground of that man's fitness for citizenship... We can not afford to consider whether he is Catholic or Protestant, Jew or Gentile; whether he is Englishman or Irishman, Frenchman or German, Japanese, Italian, or Scandinavian or Magyar. What we should desire to find out is the individual quality of the individual man...<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Minorities_and_Civil_Rights">Minorities and Civil Rights</span></h4>
<p>He was the first president to appoint a Jewish cabinet member—Secretary of Commerce and Labor, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Straus_(politician)" title="Oscar Straus (politician)">Oscar Solomon Straus</a>, who served from 1906 to 1909. Straus, who had helped co-found the Immigration Protective League in 1898, was the Roosevelt Administration's cabinet official overseeing immigration; he helped secure the passage and implementation of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Immigration_Act_of_1907&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Immigration Act of 1907 (page does not exist)">U.S. Immigration Act of 1907</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1886, Roosevelt criticized the morals of Indians he had seen:</p>
<dl>
<dd>I don't go so far as to think that the only good Indians are dead Indians, but I believe nine out of ten are, and I shouldn't like to inquire too closely into the case of the tenth. The most vicious cowboy has more moral principle than the average Indian. Turn three hundred low families of New York into New Jersey, support them for fifty years in vicious idleness, and you will have some idea of what the Indians are. Reckless, revengeful, fiendishly cruel, they rob and murder, not the cowboys, who can take care of themselves, but the defenseless, lone settlers on the plains.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Regarding African-Americans, Roosevelt told a civil rights leader:</p>
<dl>
<dd>I have not been able to think out any solution of the terrible problem offered by the presence of the Negro on this continent, but of one thing I am sure, and that is that inasmuch as he is here and can neither be killed nor driven away, the only wise and honorable and Christian thing to do is to treat each black man and each white man strictly on his merits as a man, giving him no more and no less than he shows himself worthy to have.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Roosevelt appointed numerous African Americans to federal offices, such as <a href="/wiki/Walter_L._Cohen" title="Walter L. Cohen">Walter L. Cohen</a> of New Orleans, a leader of the Black and Tan Republican faction, whom Roosevelt named register of the federal land office.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Contrasting the European conquest of North America with that of Australia, Roosevelt wrote: "The natives [of Australia] were so few in number and of such a low type, that they practically offered no resistance at all, being but little more hindrance than an equal number of ferocious beasts";<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_14.5D_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_14.5D-183"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> however, the Native Americans were "the most formidable savage foes ever faced ever encountered by colonists of European stock".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_17.5D_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_17.5D-184"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> He regarded slavery as "a crime whose shortsighted folly was worse than its guilt" because it "brought hordes of African slaves, whose descendants now form immense populations in certain portions of the land".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_8.5D_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_8.5D-185"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> Contrasting the European conquest of North America with that of South Africa, Roosevelt felt that the fate of the latter's colonists would be different because, unlike the Native American, the African "neither dies out nor recedes before their advance", meaning the colonists would likely "be swallowed up in the overwhelming mass of black barbarism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_15.E2.80.936.5D_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt1889.5Bhttps:.2F.2Farchive.org.2Fstream.2Fwinningwest16roosgoog.23page.2Fn34.2Fmode.2F2up_15.E2.80.936.5D-186"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_suicide_and_eugenics">Race suicide and eugenics</span></h4>
<p>Roosevelt was intensely active in warning against race suicide, and held a <a href="/wiki/Lamarckism#Neo-Lamarckism" title="Lamarckism">Neo-Lamarkist</a> viewpoint.<sup id="cite_ref-IdeaOfRace_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IdeaOfRace-187"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> When Roosevelt used the word 'race', he meant the entirety of the <i>human race</i> or Americans as one race and culture. Americans, he repeatedly said, were getting too soft and having too few children and were thus dying out. While he agreed with some of the ideas of eugenics, he strongly opposed the core eugenics movement principle that some people should have fewer children. As historian Thomas Dyer explains, Roosevelt, "Strenuously dissented from the ideas which contravened the race suicide..... And categorically rejected <i>any</i> measure which would not produce enough children to maintain racial integrity and national preeminence."<sup id="cite_ref-IdeaOfRace_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IdeaOfRace-187"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt attacked the fundamental axioms of eugenics, warning against "twisted eugenics".<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1914 he said: "I wish very much that the wrong people could be prevented entirely from breeding; and when the evil nature of these people is sufficiently flagrant, this should be done. Criminals should be sterilized and feeble-minded persons forbidden to leave offspring behind them."<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Madison_Grant" title="Madison Grant">Madison Grant</a> published his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Passing_of_the_Great_Race" title="The Passing of the Great Race">The Passing of the Great Race</a></i>, Roosevelt wrote this to <a href="/wiki/Scribner%27s_Magazine" title="Scribner's Magazine">Scribner's Magazine</a> to promote it:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The book is a capital book; in purpose, in vision, in grasp of the facts our people most need to realize. It shows an extraordinary range of reading and a wide scholarship. It shows a habit of singular serious thought on the subject of most commanding importance. It shows a fine fearlessness in assailing the popular and mischievous sentimentalities and attractive and corroding falsehoods which few men dare assail. It is the work of an American scholar and gentleman; and all Americans should be sincerely grateful to you for writing it.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Roosevelt was greatly impressed by the performance of ethnic American soldiers in the world war. Biographer Kathleen Dalton says:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>He insisted to [Madison] Grant that race and ethnicity did not matter because men of foreign parentage across the nation fought well, including Jews....Roosevelt took the final step toward believing in racial equality. At the end of his life TR repudiated the Madison Grants and other racists and promised W.E.B. DuBois to work with more energy for racial justice.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writer">Writer</span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_-_Address_to_the_Boys_Progressive_League.ogg" title="File:Roosevelt - Address to the Boys Progressive League.ogg">Address to the Boys Progressive League</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" style="width:220px" poster="/static/1.27.0-wmf.2/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" controls="" preload="none" class="kskin" data-durationhint="91.266666666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Roosevelt_-_Address_to_the_Boys_Progressive_League.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Roosevelt_-_Address_to_the_Boys_Progressive_League.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (97 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="96583"></source>Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.<br />
You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Roosevelt_-_Address_to_the_Boys_Progressive_League.ogg">download the clip</a> or <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">download a player</a> to play the clip in your browser.</audio></div>
</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">A speech by Roosevelt as a former President</div>
</div>
<hr /></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Roosevelt was a prolific author, writing with passion on subjects ranging from foreign policy to the importance of the national park system. Roosevelt was also an avid reader of <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>. Poet <a href="/wiki/Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost">Robert Frost</a> said that Roosevelt "was our kind. He quoted poetry to me. He knew poetry."<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As an editor of <i>Outlook</i> magazine, Roosevelt had weekly access to a large, educated national audience. In all, Roosevelt wrote about 18 books (each in several editions), including his autobiography,<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The Rough Riders</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>History of the Naval War of 1812</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> and others on subjects such as ranching, explorations, and wildlife. His most ambitious book was the four volume narrative <i>The Winning of the West</i>, focused on the <a href="/wiki/American_frontier" title="American frontier">American frontier</a> in the 18th and early 19th centuries. Roosevelt said that the American character – indeed a new "American race" (ethnic group) had emerged from the heroic wilderness hunters and Indian fighters, acting on the frontier with little government help.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt also published an account of his 1909-10 African expedition entitled <i>African Game Trails</i>.</p>
<p>In 1907, Roosevelt became embroiled in a widely publicized literary debate known as the <a href="/wiki/Nature_fakers_controversy" title="Nature fakers controversy">nature fakers controversy</a>. A few years earlier, naturalist <a href="/wiki/John_Burroughs" title="John Burroughs">John Burroughs</a> had published an article entitled "Real and Sham Natural History" in the <i>Atlantic Monthly</i>, attacking popular writers of the day such as <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Thompson_Seton" title="Ernest Thompson Seton">Ernest Thompson Seton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._D._Roberts" title="Charles G. D. Roberts">Charles G. D. Roberts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_J._Long" title="William J. Long">William J. Long</a> for their fantastical representations of wildlife. Roosevelt agreed with Burroughs' criticisms, and published several essays of his own denouncing the booming genre of "naturalistic" animal stories as "yellow journalism of the woods". It was the President himself who popularized the negative term "nature faker" to describe writers who depicted their animal characters with excessive anthropomorphism.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Character_and_beliefs">Character and beliefs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG/220px-TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG/330px-TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG/440px-TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TeddyRoosevelHouse.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sagamore Hill, Roosevelt's estate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt intensely disliked being called "Teddy", and was quick to point out this fact to those who referred to him as such, though it would become widely used by newspapers during his political career. He attended church regularly. In 1907, concerning the motto "In God We Trust" on money, he wrote, "It seems to me eminently unwise to cheapen such a motto by use on coins, just as it would be to cheapen it by use on postage stamps, or in advertisements." He was also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Freemasons" title="Freemasons" class="mw-redirect">Freemasons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_the_American_Revolution" title="Sons of the American Revolution">Sons of the American Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt had a lifelong interest in pursuing what he called, in an 1899 speech, "<a href="/wiki/The_Strenuous_Life" title="The Strenuous Life">The Strenuous Life</a>". To this end, he exercised regularly and took up boxing, tennis, hiking, rowing, polo, and horseback riding. As governor of New York, he boxed with sparring partners several times each week, a practice he regularly continued as President until being hit so hard in the face he became blind in his left eye (a fact not made public until many years later). Thereafter, he practiced judo, attaining a third degree brown belt; he also continued his habit of <a href="/wiki/Skinny-dipping" title="Skinny-dipping" class="mw-redirect">skinny-dipping</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a> during the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThayer1919Chapter_XVII.2C_pp._22.E2.80.9324_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThayer1919Chapter_XVII.2C_pp._22.E2.80.9324-201"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt was an enthusiastic <a href="/wiki/Singlestick" title="Singlestick">singlestick</a> player and, according to <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Weekly" title="Harper's Weekly">Harper's Weekly</a></i>, showed up at a White House reception with his arm bandaged after a bout with General <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Wood" title="Leonard Wood">Leonard Wood</a> in 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was an avid reader, reading tens of thousands of books, at a rate of several per day in multiple languages. Along with Thomas Jefferson, Roosevelt was the most well-read of all American politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>Historians credit Roosevelt for changing the nation's political system by permanently placing the presidency at center stage and making character as important as the issues. His notable accomplishments include trust busting and conservationism. He is a hero to liberals for his proposals in 1907–12 that presaged the modern welfare state of the New Deal Era, and put the environment on the national agenda. Conservatives admire his "big stick" diplomacy and commitment to military values. Dalton says, "Today he is heralded as the architect of the modern presidency, as a world leader who boldly reshaped the office to meet the needs of the new century and redefined America's place in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalton20024.E2.80.935_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalton20024.E2.80.935-205"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>However, liberals have criticized him for his interventionist and imperialist approach to nations he considered "uncivilized". Conservatives reject his vision of the welfare state and emphasis on the superiority of government over private action. Historians typically rank Roosevelt among the top five presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persona_and_masculinity">Persona and masculinity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ROLES2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/ROLES2.JPG/300px-ROLES2.JPG" width="300" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/ROLES2.JPG/450px-ROLES2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/ROLES2.JPG/600px-ROLES2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1435" data-file-height="718" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ROLES2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1910 cartoon shows Roosevelt's multiple roles from 1899 to 1910</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dalton says Roosevelt is remembered as, "one of the most picturesque personalities who has ever enlivened the landscape".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalton20025_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalton20025-208"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> His friend, historian <a href="/wiki/Henry_Adams" title="Henry Adams">Henry Adams</a> proclaimed</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Roosevelt, more than any other man... showed the singular primitive quality that belongs to ultimate matter—the quality that medieval theology assigned to God—he was pure act.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Recent biographers have stressed Roosevelt's personality. Cooper compared him with Woodrow Wilson, and discovered that both of them played the roles of warrior and priest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1983_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1983-210"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> Dalton stressed Roosevelt's strenuous life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalton2002_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalton2002-211"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> Sarah Watts examined the desires of the "Rough Rider in the White House".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatts2003_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatts2003-212"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> Brands calls Roosevelt "the last romantic", arguing that his romantic concept of life emerged from his belief that "physical bravery was the highest virtue and war the ultimate test of bravery".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997x_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997x-213"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt as the exemplar of American masculinity has become a major theme.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETesti1995_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETesti1995-214"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> As president, he repeatedly warned men that they were becoming too office-bound, too complacent, too comfortable with physical ease and moral laxity, and were failing in their duties to propagate the race and exhibit masculine vigor.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> French historian Serge Ricard says, "the ebullient apostle of the Strenuous Life offers ideal material for a detailed psycho-historical analysis of aggressive manhood in the changing socio-cultural environment of his era; McKinley, Taft, or Wilson would perhaps inadequately serve that purpose".<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> He promoted competitive sports and the <a href="/wiki/Boy_Scouts_of_America" title="Boy Scouts of America">Boy Scouts of America</a>, founded in 1910, as the way forward.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> Brands shows that heroic displays of bravery were essential to Roosevelt's image and mission:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What makes the hero a hero is the romantic notion that he stands above the tawdry give and take of everyday politics, occupying an ethereal realm where partisanship gives way to patriotism, and division to unity, and where the nation regains its lost innocence, and the people their shared sense of purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands1997372_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands1997372-219"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg/175px-MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg" width="175" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg/263px-MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg/350px-MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg 2x" data-file-width="707" data-file-height="875" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MtRushmore_TR_close.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt's face on Mount Rushmore</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg/150px-Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg/225px-Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg/300px-Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_in_Youngs_Memorial_Cemetery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt's Grave, Oyster Bay, NY</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt was included with Presidents <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rushmore" title="Mount Rushmore">Mount Rushmore Memorial</a>, designed in 1927 with the approval of Republican President <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> For his gallantry at San Juan Hill, Roosevelt's commanders recommended him for the <a href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor" title="Medal of Honor">Medal of Honor</a>. In the late 1990s, Roosevelt's supporters again recommended the award. On January 16, 2001, President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> awarded Theodore Roosevelt the Medal of Honor posthumously for his charge on San Juan Hill, Cuba, during the Spanish–American War.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> named two ships for Roosevelt: the <a href="/wiki/USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_(SSBN-600)" title="USS Theodore Roosevelt (SSBN-600)">USS&#160;<i>Theodore Roosevelt</i>&#160;(SSBN-600)</a>, a submarine that was in commission from 1961 to 1982, and the <a href="/wiki/USS_Theodore_Roosevelt_(CVN-71)" title="USS Theodore Roosevelt (CVN-71)">USS&#160;<i>Theodore Roosevelt</i>&#160;(CVN-71)</a>, an aircraft carrier that has been on active duty in the Atlantic Fleet since 1986. On November 18, 1956, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> released a 6¢ <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Issue" title="Liberty Issue">Liberty Issue</a> postage stamp honoring Roosevelt. A 32¢ stamp was issued on February 3, 1998, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Celebrate_the_Century" title="Celebrate the Century">Celebrate the Century</a> stamp sheet series.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, Columbia Law School awarded a law degree to Roosevelt, posthumously making him a member of the class of 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-cls20080925_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cls20080925-224"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> In Chicago, the city renamed 12th Street to <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Road" title="Roosevelt Road">Roosevelt Road</a> four months after Roosevelt's death.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theodore_Roosevelt_Association">Theodore Roosevelt Association</span></h3>
<p>In 1919, the <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Association" title="Theodore Roosevelt Association">Theodore Roosevelt Association</a> (originally known as the Permanent Memorial National Committee) was founded by friends and supporters of Roosevelt. Soon renamed the Roosevelt Memorial Association (RMA), it was chartered in 1920 under Title 36 of the United States Code. In parallel with the RMA was an organization for women, The Women's Theodore Roosevelt Association, that had been founded in 1919 by an act of the New York State Assembly. Both organizations merged in 1956 under the current name. This organization preserved Roosevelt's papers in a 20-year project, preserved his photos and established four public sites: the reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Birthplace_National_Historic_Site" title="Theodore Roosevelt Birthplace National Historic Site">Theodore Roosevelt Birthplace National Historic Site</a>, New York City, dedicated in 1923 and donated to the National Park Service in 1963; <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Memorial_Park" title="Theodore Roosevelt Memorial Park">Theodore Roosevelt Memorial Park</a>, Oyster Bay, Long Island, New York, dedicated in 1928 and given to the people of Oyster Bay; <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Island" title="Theodore Roosevelt Island">Theodore Roosevelt Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, given to the federal government in 1932; <a href="/wiki/Sagamore_Hill_(house)" title="Sagamore Hill (house)">Sagamore Hill (house)</a>, Roosevelt's Oyster Bay home, opened to the public in 1953 and was donated to the National Park Service in 1963 and is now the Sagamore Hill National Historic Site. The organization has its own web site at <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.theodoreroosevelt.org">http://www.theodoreroosevelt.org</a> and maintains a Facebook page at <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.facebook.com/pages/Theodore-Roosevelt-Association/41852696878">https://www.facebook.com/pages/Theodore-Roosevelt-Association/41852696878</a>.</p>
<p>Other locations named for Roosevelt include <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_National_Park" title="Theodore Roosevelt National Park">Theodore Roosevelt National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Lake" title="Theodore Roosevelt Lake">Theodore Roosevelt Lake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Dam" title="Theodore Roosevelt Dam">Theodore Roosevelt Dam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg/220px-Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg/330px-Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg/440px-Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="514" data-file-height="334" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joe_Wiegand_TR_WhiteHouse_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
TR impersonator <a href="/wiki/Joe_Wiegand" title="Joe Wiegand">Joe Wiegand</a> performs October 27, 2008 in the East Room of the White House, during a celebration of Roosevelt's 150th birthday.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt's "<a href="/wiki/Big_Stick_ideology" title="Big Stick ideology">Speak Softly and Carry a Big Stick</a>" ideology is still quoted by politicians and columnists in different countries—not only in English, but also in translations to various other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Kelley_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley-91"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One lasting, popular legacy of Roosevelt is the stuffed toy bears—<a href="/wiki/Teddy_bear" title="Teddy bear">teddy bears</a>—named after him following an incident on a hunting trip in Mississippi in 1902. Roosevelt famously refused to shoot a defenseless <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">black bear</a>. After the cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Clifford_K._Berryman" title="Clifford K. Berryman">Clifford K. Berryman</a> illustrated the President with a bear, a toy maker heard the story and named the teddy bear after Roosevelt. Bears, and later bear cubs, became closely associated with Roosevelt in political cartoons, despite Roosevelt openly despising being called "Teddy".<sup id="cite_ref-HandyBook_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HandyBook-80"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> On June 26, 2006, Roosevelt was on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">TIME</a></i> magazine with the lead story, "The Making of America—Theodore Roosevelt—The 20th Century Express": "At home and abroad, Theodore Roosevelt was the locomotive President, the man who drew his flourishing nation into the future."<sup id="cite_ref-natfut_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natfut-226"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg/220px-Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg/330px-Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg/440px-Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2504" data-file-height="3505" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodore_Roosevelt_on_horseback.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Roosevelt. Pastel study by V. Floyd Campbell after a snapshot photograph. (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">?1903</span>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1905, Roosevelt, an admirer of various western figures, named <a href="/wiki/Captain_Bill_McDonald" title="Captain Bill McDonald" class="mw-redirect">Captain Bill McDonald</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Ranger_Division" title="Texas Ranger Division">Texas Rangers</a> as his bodyguard and entertained the legendary Texan at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. Ironically, in the 1912 campaign, McDonald was Woodrow Wilson's bodyguard. Wilson thereafter named the Democrat McDonald as the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Marshal" title="U.S. Marshal" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Marshal</a> for the Northern district of Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt has been portrayed many times in film and on <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Swenson" title="Karl Swenson">Karl Swenson</a> played him in the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">western</a> picture <i><a href="/wiki/Brighty_of_the_Grand_Canyon" title="Brighty of the Grand Canyon">Brighty of the Grand Canyon</a></i>, the story of a real-life <a href="/wiki/Burro" title="Burro">burro</a> who guided Roosevelt on a hunting trip to find <a href="/wiki/Mountain_lion" title="Mountain lion" class="mw-redirect">mountain lions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brian_Keith" title="Brian Keith">Brian Keith</a> played Roosevelt in the 1975 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wind_and_the_Lion" title="The Wind and the Lion">The Wind and the Lion</a></i>. He was also portrayed by actor <a href="/wiki/Tom_Berenger" title="Tom Berenger">Tom Berenger</a> in 1997 for the TNT movie <i><a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders_(film)" title="Rough Riders (film)" class="mw-redirect">Rough Riders</a></i>, a made-for-cable film about his exploits during the Spanish–American War in Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Albertson" title="Frank Albertson">Frank Albertson</a> played Roosevelt in the episode "Rough and Ready" of the <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/My_Friend_Flicka_(TV_series)" title="My Friend Flicka (TV series)">My Friend Flicka</a>.</i>"<i><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup></i> <a href="/wiki/Robin_Williams" title="Robin Williams">Robin Williams</a> portrayed Roosevelt in the form of a wax <a href="/wiki/Mannequin" title="Mannequin">mannequin</a> that comes to life in <i><a href="/wiki/Night_at_the_Museum" title="Night at the Museum">Night at the Museum</a></i> and its sequels <i><a href="/wiki/Night_at_the_Museum:_Battle_of_the_Smithsonian" title="Night at the Museum: Battle of the Smithsonian">Night at the Museum: Battle of the Smithsonian</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Night_at_the_Museum:_Secret_of_the_Tomb" title="Night at the Museum: Secret of the Tomb">Night at the Museum: Secret of the Tomb</a></i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Theodore Roosevelt was one of the first presidents whose voice was recorded for posterity. Several of his recorded speeches survive.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> A 4.6-minute voice recording,<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> which preserves Roosevelt's lower timbre ranges particularly well for its time, is among those available from the Michigan State University libraries (this is the 1912 recording of <i>The Right of the People to Rule</i>, recorded by Edison at Carnegie Hall). The audio clip sponsored by the Authentic History Center includes his defense<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> of the Progressive Party in 1912, wherein he proclaims it the "party of the people", in contrast with the other major parties.</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His last name is, according to Roosevelt himself, "pronounced as if it was spelled 'Rosavelt.' That is in three syllables. The first syllable as if it was 'Rose.'"<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies_2">Domestic policies</span></h3>
<div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul>
